Parsing Guide for the Treebank Semantics Parsed Corpus (TSPC)

Alastair Butler

Hirosaki University




Table of contents

  • Chapter 1 Grammar and Parsing
  • Chapter 2 Words

Chapter 1
Grammar and Parsing


1.1   Introduction

The term GRAMMAR refers to the system (mechanism or otherwise) for building language expressions. When something is built, there is the natural impulse to want to take it apart. Taking apart language is known as PARSING. With care, we can put dismantled parts back together, and while doing so we can also add trails of instructions known as annotations. Such enhancements to language data act as handles to access the structural analysis revealed by parsing. This book is focused on introducing the annotation approach of the Treebank Semantics Parsed Corpus (TSPC). The TSPC is a corpus of English for general use with hand worked analysis for approaching half-a-million words.

1.1.1   A hierarchy of layers

With parsing being the activity of taking apart language expressions, the aim is to reach smaller and smaller layers of grammatical analysis. To start, let's consider layers of structure introduced in terms of following possible paths through Figure 1.1 from the highest sentence layer through to the lowest word layers, via clause and possibly phrase layers, with the arrow X ⟶ Y interpreted: ‘X immediately contains Y’.

Figure 1.1: Paths through layers of grammatical analysis

    A SENTENCE is at the top of the hierarchy, so it is the largest unit considered. (A discourse grammar would look beyond the sentence layer.) A WORD is at the other end of the hierarchy, being of two kinds: either a VERB (and so immediately contained within a clause) or a -VERB (non-verb word). (It is also possible to go inside words to consider morphology, that is, the components from which words are made, which will go lower still in the hierarchy of structure.)

    As an intermediate layer in the hierarchy of Figure 1.1, a CLAUSE is reached from the sentence layer, and subsequently a PHRASE can be reached from a clause layer. In addition, clauses can occur within clauses, phrases can occur within phrases, and clauses can occur within phrases.

1.1.2   Identifying sentence and word layers

Sentence and word layers are fairly easily identified by conventions of the English writing system: Sentences are delimited by an initial capital letter and a final full-stop (or question-mark or exclamative-mark); Words are created with single character strings that are delimited by a space on each side (or punctuation mark other than a hyphen or apostrophe). But these conventions are not always followed. An important exception is when a sequence of words behaves syntactically in an idiomatic way (e.g. ‘as long as’, ‘with regard to’, ‘by the way’). Such word sequences will be analysed as if single words, with each component separated by underscore characters in a single character string, as in (1.1).

(1.1)
as_long_as
with_regard_to
by_the_way

1.2   A simple sentence

A simple sentence consists of one clause, so Figure 1.1 paths will start: "SENTENCE","CLAUSE". This initial clause layer is known as the MATRIX clause, and is symbolised most generally with IP-MAT. IP is an abbreviation for Inflectional Phrase, which is another way to say ‘clause’. A clause contains minimally a verb. Consider the simple sentence (1.2), which is comprised of a single verb contained within a single clause.

(1.2)
Look!

This can be given the paths of Figure 1.2 from sentence layer to words, with the punctuation treated as a word.

Figure 1.2: Paths through ‘Look!’

The parse information of Figure 1.2 can be represented with bracketed notation as in (1.3), which follows CorpusSearch format (Randall 2009).

(1.3)
( (IP-MAT (VERB Look)
          (-VERB !))
  (ID 101_classics_SAINT_EXUPERY_1943))

With CorpusSearch format, every tree has a “wrapper”. A wrapper is a pair of unlabelled parentheses surrounding the tree content together with an ID node. Furthermore, for the TSPC, an ID node contains a character string that begins with a number corresponding to the number of the tree in its corpus file. This tree number is followed by an underscore (‘_’) and then by the name of the corpus file (minus any extension). Other character string material may follow the filename, provided there is an intervening semicolon (‘;’).

    Another way to present the parse information from sentence layer to words is with (1.4).

(1.4)
=N(" Look ! ")
"IP-MAT","VERB","Look"
"IP-MAT","-VERB","!"
"ID","101_classics_SAINT_EXUPERY_1943"

With (1.4), each word of the parse comes at the end of its own line, with each line presenting path information from the root (IP-MAT) layer through to a word layer. The last line is the ID node for the parse.


1.3   A complex sentence

Consider (1.5) as an example of the interplay between intermediate clause and phrase layers.

(1.5)
He wanted to show what happened to anyone who tried to start trouble.

Analysis of (1.5) leads to the bracketed structure of (1.6).

(1.6)
( (IP-MAT (PHRASE (-VERB He))
          (VERB wanted)
          (CLAUSE (-VERB to)
                  (VERB show)
                  (CLAUSE (PHRASE (-VERB what))
                          (VERB happened)
                          (PHRASE (-VERB to)
                                  (PHRASE (-VERB anyone)
                                          (CLAUSE (PHRASE (-VERB who))
                                                  (VERB tried)
                                                  (CLAUSE (-VERB to)
                                                          (VERB start)
                                                          (PHRASE (-VERB trouble))))))))
          (-VERB .))
  (ID 154_susanne_N12))

When viewed in terms of paths from sentence layer to words, the analysis seen with (1.6) becomes (1.7).

(1.7)
=N(" He wanted to show what happened to anyone who tried to start trouble . ")
"IP-MAT","PHRASE","-VERB","He"
"IP-MAT","VERB","wanted"
"IP-MAT","CLAUSE","-VERB","to"
"IP-MAT","CLAUSE","VERB","show"
"IP-MAT","CLAUSE","CLAUSE","PHRASE;@1","-VERB","what"
"IP-MAT","CLAUSE","CLAUSE","VERB","happened"
"IP-MAT","CLAUSE","CLAUSE","PHRASE;@3","-VERB","to"
"IP-MAT","CLAUSE","CLAUSE","PHRASE;@3","PHRASE","-VERB","anyone"
"IP-MAT","CLAUSE","CLAUSE","PHRASE;@3","PHRASE","CLAUSE","PHRASE","-VERB","who"
"IP-MAT","CLAUSE","CLAUSE","PHRASE;@3","PHRASE","CLAUSE","VERB","tried"
"IP-MAT","CLAUSE","CLAUSE","PHRASE;@3","PHRASE","CLAUSE","CLAUSE","-VERB","to"
"IP-MAT","CLAUSE","CLAUSE","PHRASE;@3","PHRASE","CLAUSE","CLAUSE","VERB","start"
"IP-MAT","CLAUSE","CLAUSE","PHRASE;@3","PHRASE","CLAUSE","CLAUSE","PHRASE","-VERB","trouble"
"IP-MAT","-VERB","."
"ID","154_susanne_N12"

    The paths of (1.7) conform to Figure 1.1. They start with a sentence and clause layer (IP-MAT), and then can have clauses within clauses, phrases within clauses, phrases within phrases, clauses within phrases, verbs with occasional non-verb words within clauses, and non-verb words within phrases.

    Note that in (1.7) there are nodes with extra marking: "PHRASE;@1" and "PHRASE;@3". These nodes have the same preceding path: "IP-MAT","CLAUSE","CLAUSE". When distinct nodes have the same tag and the same preceding path, extra marking ensures they are uniquely distinguished when considering multiple paths. The convention followed for extra marking includes ‘;@n’ for the n-th sister node of a shared preceding path.


1.4   Orientation

Each of the units of structure that label the brackets of (1.3) and (1.6), and paths of (1.4) and (1.7), is either a clause or a phrase. It is typical to refer to such units as CONSTITUENTS.

    We have so far categorised words into being either:

  • VERB words (e.g. look, wanted, start), or
  • -VERB words (e.g. he, to, town).

Because of this categorisation, we have already been able to distinguish clause constituents from phrase constituents:

  • An immediately contained VERB word gives a clause layer.
  • Without an immediately contained VERB word, a non-word unit is a phrase.

    We will want to further distinguish phrases into being of various kinds based on the words they contain. To this end, we can divide -VERB words into classes such as:

  • noun (N),
  • adjective (ADJ), and
  • adverb (ADV).

With such divisions, phrases can be sub-divided into:

  • noun phrases (NP) that minimally contain a noun,
  • adjective phrases (ADJP) that minimally contain an adjective, and
  • adverb phrases (ADVP) that minimally contain an adverb.

    Establishing the range of word classes is the aim of chapter 2. Looking at phrase classes in detail is pursued in chapter 3. A big part of chapters 2 and 3 will be to raise and answer questions of FORM, that is, how words and constituents look — their shape or appearance, and what their internal structure happens to be. But form is not the full story required for parsing: In order to gain motivation for having structure, we will often need to be equally concerned to explore the FUNCTION that words and constituents perform in the larger structures which contain them.

    The dual concern for form and function in chapters 2 and 3 will be carried over to an exploration of clause constituents in chapter 4. Chapter 5 details options available for realising complex sentences with clause-within-clause subordination.

    The parsing practise developed over chapters 3, 4, and 5 will involve specifying form and function for the constituents named PHRASE and CLAUSE above. IP-MAT as a constituent name may also need further specification. For example, the annotation of (1.3)/(1.4) can be further refined with IP-MAT-IMP replacing IP-MAT to identify sentence (1.2) as an imperative.

    Chapter 6 integrates verb codes that signal the clause requirements of main verbs.

    Chapter 7 adds the use of indexing and other practises that broaden the kinds of connections that the annotation can capture.

1.4.1   Notational conventions

Useful notational conventions used in this book are detailed in Table 1.1.

Table 1.1: Notational conventions

NotationUnitExample
italics word-form the word look
SMALL CAPITALS lexeme the verb lexeme LOOK (look, looks, looked, looking)
{...} morpheme the suffix {s}
TELETYPE annotation the matrix clause tag label IP-MAT

1.5   About the annotation approach

This book's annotation approach builds on alternative annotation schemes for English. A central goal has been to consolidate strengths of existing methods in a single annotation scheme with a high degree of normalised structure. In particular, ideas were taken from the following annotation schemes:

  • The SUSANNE Corpus and Analytic Scheme (Sampson 1995)
  • The ICE Parsing Scheme (Nelson, Wallis, and Aarts 2002)
  • The Penn Treebank Scheme (Marcus, Santorini, and Marcinkiewicz 1993)
  • The Penn Historical Parsed Corpora Scheme (Santorini 2010)

    From the SUSANNE scheme, there is adoption of form and function information, such that the approach taken in this book can be linked most closely to the SUSANNE scheme. Moreover, a large percentage (nearly three quarters) of the Treebank Semantics Parsed Corpus (TSPC) exists as data that was converted from annotation that had been following the SUSANNE scheme.

    Regarding form and function information, the SUSANNE scheme is closely related to the English grammars of Quirk et al. (1972, 1985). As a consequence, there is compatibility with many other presentations of English grammar, particularly work with pedagogic aims. This includes Leech et al. (1982), which influenced the presentation of materials within this book.

    The ICE Parsing Scheme similarly follows the Quirk et al. grammars. In addition, ICE is notable for its rich range of features, and care has been taken to ensure that the current annotation supports the ability for many of these features to be automatically derived.

    The Penn Historical Corpora scheme, which itself draws on the bracketed approach of the Penn Treebank scheme, has informed the ‘look’ of the annotation. This includes:

(i)
the tag labelling: CP and IP for clause layers; ADJP, ADVP, NP, and PP for phrase layers,
(ii)
that label extensions are added to CP, IP, ADJP, ADVP, NP, and PP to indicate functions, and
(iii)
the presentation of conjunction structure with CONJP layers.

However, it should be noted that, labels aside, the tagset of the current scheme is most compatible with the SUSANNE scheme, especially with regards to function marking. Moreover, word class tags closely overlap with the Lancaster word class tagging systems, especially the UCREL CLAWS7 Tagset used for the British National Corpus (BNC-Consortium 2005).

    The annotation also contains plenty that is innovative. For example, normalised structure is achieved with intermediate layers at:

  • clause levels (IML),
  • noun phrase levels (NML), and
  • adjective phrase and adverb phrase levels (AML).

These intermediate layers are required when their corresponding levels of structure include CONJP layers.

    Another area of innovation is the verb code integration of chapter 6. This adopts the verb code system of the fourth edition of the Oxford Advanced Learner's Dictionary (OALD4; Cowie 1989). Extra codes with with very particular distributions are included from Hornby (1975). Also, there are codes to classify catenative verbs. These catenative codes are relatable to the analysis of catenative verbs in Huddleston and Pullum (2002), and are supported in the annotation by the layering of clause structure.

    The most innovative aspect of the annotation gives the TSPC its name: It can be fed to the Treebank Semantics evaluation system (Butler 2021). Treebank Semantics processes constituency tree annotations and returns logic based meaning representations. While the annotation seldom includes indexing, results calculated with Treebank Semantics resolve both inter and intra clause dependencies, including cross sentential anaphoric dependencies from a discourse perspective. It is sometimes especially helpful to see meaning representations from Treebank Semantics as dependency graphs (e.g., from the on-line corpus interface) to make visually apparent connections that the design of the annotation captures.





Chapter 2
Words


2.1   Introduction

This chapter details the range of possible words as the bottom layer of sentence structure, while chapter 3 considers phrase layers, and chapter 4 ascends to clause layers. This unravelling of the layers of grammatical structure will show all elements as inter-related components. Understanding of what is possible at one layer will require the integration of knowledge from the others. With this chapter, words are categorised into classes informed by how words function in phrases and clauses.

    Word analysis involves saying with a TAG the WORD CLASS to which a given word in context belongs. This task is known as TAGGING. One criteria for tagging is to identify words that can be minimal components for phrase layers: words for noun phrases are discussed in section 2.2; words for adjective phrases, in section 2.3; and words for adverb phrases, in section 2.4. Section 2.5 covers other word types that appear under a noun phrase as modifiers. Section 2.6 presents tags for verbs which are components for clause layers. Section 2.7 adds other word types that occur under a clause layer. Section 2.8 considers words that serve as connectives to combine layers. Section 2.9 looks at the treatment of punctuation. Section 2.10 deals with interjection, reaction signals, and formulaic expressions. Section 2.11 notes other possible elements of a parse.

2.1.1   Beware: Same character string; different word class tag

Section 1.1.2 identifies words through the conventions of the English writing system as character strings delimited by a space on each side, while noting exceptions. Word identification is not a complete word analysis: The same character string might be used as a word in different ways in different contexts. Tagging completes the word analysis. For example, down in (2.1) occurs as a noun (N), adjective (ADJ), verb (VB), adverb particle (RP), and preposition (P-ROLE).

(2.1)
a.
(VBP filled) (P-ROLE with) (ADJ pure) (N down)
b.
(PRO she) (BED was) (ADV very) (ADJ down)
c.
(PRO he) (VBD started) (TO to) (VB down) (D the) (N whisky)
d.
(VBP tear) (RP down) (D that) (N wall)
e.
(PRO You) (MD may) (VB look) (ADV back) (P-ROLE down) (D the) (N mountain)

The examples of (2.1) show how there might be different tags for what are different instances in different contexts of the same character string. Tag differences due to context can happen even within the same sentence. For example, that in (2.2) occurs as a complementizer (C), determiner (D), noun (N), and relative pronoun (RPRO).

(2.2)
=N(" I think that that that that that boy ate was a hamburger . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
"IP-MAT","VBP;~Tf","think"
"IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","C","that"
"IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","D","that"
"IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","N","that"
"IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","IP-REL","NP-OB1","RPRO","that"
"IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","IP-REL","NP-SBJ","D","that"
"IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","IP-REL","NP-SBJ","N","boy"
"IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","IP-REL","VBP;~Tn","ate"
"IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","BED;~Ln","was"
"IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","NP-PRD2","D","a"
"IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","NP-PRD2","N","hamburger"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","56_samples_BUFFALO@9,12,14,18,21"

Yet another possible tag for that is as a prepositional connective (P-CONN), as seen in (2.3).

(2.3)
=N(" <ldquo> tell me <comma> that I might take my big sword and slay him . <rdquo> ")
"IP-MAT-IMP","PUQ","<ldquo>"
"IP-MAT-IMP","VB","tell"
"IP-MAT-IMP","NP-OB1;{GIANT}","PRO","me"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PU","<comma>"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PP-SCON-CNT","P-CONN","that"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-ADV","NP-SBJ;{GIANT}","PRO","I"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-ADV","MD","might"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-ADV","IML","IML","VB","take"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-ADV","IML","IML","NP-OB1","NP-GENV;{GIANT}","PRO","my"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-ADV","IML","IML","NP-OB1","ADJP","ADJ","big"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-ADV","IML","IML","NP-OB1","N","sword"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-ADV","IML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-ADV","IML","CONJP","IML","VB","slay"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-ADV","IML","CONJP","IML","NP-OB1","PRO","him"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PU","."
"IP-MAT-IMP","PUQ","<rdquo>"
"ID","90_classics_WILDE_2_1888@12"

2.2   Words that can head a noun phrase

Certain words form the minimal component required for the phrase layers that immediately contain them. Such a minimal component is called the HEAD of the phrase. The word class tags of Table 2.1 distinguish words that can head a noun phrase (NP; see section 3.2).

Table 2.1: Tags for words that can head a noun phrase

NSPlural common noun (e.g. children, revelations, times, wishes)
NCommon noun not subclassified as NS, that is, either singular (e.g. child, revelation, time, wish), or neutral for number (e.g. committee, fish, information)
NPRSPlural proper noun (e.g. Heathers, Koreas)
NPRProper noun not subclassified as NPRS, that is either singular (e.g. Heather, Tokyo), or neutral for number (e.g. Andes, IBM)
PNXReflexive pronoun (e.g. myself, yourself, itself, ourselves), or reciprocal pronoun (e.g. each_other)
PROPersonal pronoun (e.g. I, you, them, ours)
RPRORelative pronoun (e.g. which, who, whom, whose, that)
WPROWh-pronoun (e.g. what, who, whom, whose)
QIndefinite pronoun with quantification, which can be a compound (e.g. everybody, nothing), or a word which often occurs with the preposition of (e.g. much, many, a_lot) — note, Q is also used for quantifiers; see section 2.5
DIndefinite pronoun not subclassified as Q (e.g. someone, anything, another), and demonstrative pronoun (e.g. this, that, these, those) — note, D is also used for indefinite and definite articles/determiners; see section 2.5

The rest of this section describes morphosyntactic properties for identifying the words of Table 2.1.

2.2.1   Suffixes that derive nouns

There are many suffixes that derive nouns from other nouns or from words of other classes. Some examples include: {age} (package, usage), {er} (officer, teacher), {ness} (illness, awareness), {ship} (championship, relationship), {tion} (action, organisation), {ty} (ability, responsibility).

2.2.2   Count nouns and plural marking

Many nouns take the suffix {(e)s} when they refer to plural items:

(2.4)
(N hedgehog)      (NS hedgehogs)
(N revelation)    (NS revelations)
(N time)          (NS times)
(N wish)          (NS wishes)
etc.

Such nouns are instances of COUNT nouns, so called, because they can combine with numerals (e.g. three wishes). A few count nouns take the irregular suffix {(r)en} in the plural:

(2.5)
(N child)         (NS children)
(N ox)            (NS oxen)

There are also count nouns that use the same form in the singular and plural. For example, nouns for the animal species in (2.6) look like singular nouns, and so are tagged N, but can also be used as plurals (e.g. three deer); while (2.7) is tagged NS for looking like a plural, but might also refer to a single pair.

(2.6)
(N deer)
(N fish)
(2.7)
(NS scissors)

2.2.3   Mass nouns

In contrast to count nouns, there are MASS (or noncount) nouns that can neither combine with numerals nor inflect for number. For example:

(2.8)
(N gold)
(N information)

It is possible for the same noun to belong to both count and mass categories: in Her hair is brown, hair is a mass noun, but in I found a hair in my soup, it is a count noun.

2.2.4   Proper nouns

Proper nouns are written with an initial capital letter. Inflection for number is rare, but is possible in talk about more than one entity with the same name, e.g. There are two Heathers in my class.

(2.9)
(NPR Heather)
(NPRS Heathers)

2.2.5   Genitive case

Both proper nouns and common nouns can be case marked — but only for genitive case — by the genitive suffix {'s} (or {'} if the noun already has the plural suffix {s}). For parsing, the genitive marking is made to form a distinct word that is tagged GENM:

Table 2.2: Tag for genitive marker

GENMThe genitive marker {'s} or {'}

This marker is placed leftmost under an NP-GENV projection that comprises the genitive content. For example:

(2.10)
=N(" Wendy <apos>s boyfriend ")
"NP-SBJ","NP-GENV","NPR","Wendy"
"NP-SBJ","NP-GENV","GENM","<apos>s"
"NP-SBJ","N","boyfriend"
"ID","4#100_bnc_B33@5"
(2.11)
=N(" a couple of hours <apos> work ")
"NP-OB1","NP-GENV","D","a"
"NP-OB1","NP-GENV","N","couple"
"NP-OB1","NP-GENV","PP","P-ROLE","of"
"NP-OB1","NP-GENV","PP","NP","NS","hours"
"NP-OB1","NP-GENV","GENM","<apos>"
"NP-OB1","N","work"
"ID","7#212_christine_T37@13"

Double marking of a genitive is possible when the content of the genitive has both a following genitive marker and a preceding of preposition, as (2.12) illustrates.

(2.12)
=N(" a friend of Lucy <apos>s ")
"NP-PRD2","D","a"
"NP-PRD2","N","friend"
"NP-PRD2","PP-GENV","P-ROLE","of"
"NP-PRD2","PP-GENV","NP","NPR","Lucy"
"NP-PRD2","PP-GENV","NP","GENM","<apos>s"
"ID","8#1_samples_file@12,6"

2.2.6   Reciprocal, reflexive, and personal pronouns

This section presents an overview of reciprocal and reflexive pronouns (PNX), and personal pronouns (PRO). Such words are understood in the context of their occurrence, often by taking already mentioned noun phrases as antecedents. They can appear in full noun phrase positions, with the range of grammatical markings detailed in Table 2.3.

Table 2.3: Reciprocal and reflexive pronouns (PNX), and personal pronouns (PRO)

1st person 2nd person 3rd person
Singular Plural Singular Plural Singular Plural
Masculine Feminine Neuter
Reciprocal (PNX) each_other, one_another
Reflexive (PNX) myself ourselves yourself yourselves himself herself itself themselves
Subject (PRO) I we you he she it they
Non-subject (PRO) me us him her them
Genitive 1 (PRO) my our your his its their
Genitive 2 (PRO) mine ours yours hers theirs

    The reciprocal pronouns each_other and one_another are used to indicate a relationship between conjoined nouns, for example:

(2.13)
=N(" John and Mary love each other . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","NML","NP","NPR","John"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","NP","NPR","Mary"
"IP-MAT","VBP","love"
"IP-MAT","NP-OB1","PNX","each_other"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","133_samples_blue_book@16"

Reflexive personal pronouns can indicate that the subject and object are the same entity, for example,

(2.14)
=N(" The facts speak for themselves . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","The"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","NS","facts"
"IP-MAT","VBP","speak"
"IP-MAT","PP-BNF","P-ROLE","for"
"IP-MAT","PP-BNF","NP","PNX","themselves"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","52_bnc_B41@13"

Reflexive forms are also used for emphasis, for example,

(2.15)
=N(" I made it all myself . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRN","*ICH*-1"
"IP-MAT","VBD;~Tn","made"
"IP-MAT","NP-OB1","PRO","it"
"IP-MAT","ADVP-MNR","ADV","all"
"IP-MAT","PRN-1","NP","PNX","myself"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","2_age_09_M73@17"

    A look across the columns of Table 2.3 shows that the category of ‘person’ is useful in differentiating the various reflexive and personal pronouns. The first person refers to the speaker(s), the second person refers to the hearer(s), and the third person refers to other entities. Person is further distinguished into singular and plural reference. Further distinction is possible with third person singular pronouns (reflexive and personal), since they inflect for gender: This gives: masculine he/him(self)/his, feminine she/her(self)/hers, and neuter it(self)/its.

    A look down the rows of Table 2.3 shows that pronouns are marked for more grammatical cases than common nouns or proper nouns. In addition to genitive case, pronouns are marked for nominative case (marking the subject of the clause: I shrugged), and accusative case (marking the object acted on by the verb: James tickled me).

(2.16)
=N(" I shrugged . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
"IP-MAT","VBD;~I","shrugged"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","50_texts_LooseChange@3,2"
(2.17)
=N(" James tickled me ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","NPR","James"
"IP-MAT","VBD","tickled"
"IP-MAT","NP-OB1","PRO","me"
"ID","91_christine_T03@8,7"

    Genitive pronouns can be either:

  • dependent (Genitive 1 of Table 2.3), i.e. standing for the possessor only, and appearing with a following noun (for example, our friends), or
(2.18)
=N(" our friends ")
"NP","NP-GENV","PRO","our"
"NP","NS","friends"
"ID","44#10_age_09_M51@3,2"
  • independent (Genitive 2 of Table 2.3), i.e. standing for the whole NP, so occurring without a following noun (for example, it is theirs all right).
(2.19)
=N(" it <apos>s theirs all right ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","it"
"IP-MAT","BEP;~Ln","<apos>s"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","NP-GENV","PRO","theirs"
"IP-MAT","ADVP-MOD","ADV","all_right"
"ID","6#468_christine_T13@9,8"

As shown by examples (2.18) and (2.19), with parsing, a genitive pronoun is a PRO tagged word that is the only element of an NP-GENV layer.

2.2.7   Other types of pronouns

Other types of pronouns found in English include:

  • Interrogative pronouns (tagged WPRO) are used in questions, and stand for the entity under question, for example, Who came?, What did it mean?
(2.20)
=N(" Who came ? ")
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","WPRO","Who"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","VBD","came"
"CP-QUE","PU","?"
"ID","712_samples_kisonihongo@4"
(2.21)
=N(" What did it mean ? ")
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","NP-OB1","WPRO","What"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","DOD","did"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","PRO","it"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","VB","mean"
"CP-QUE","PU","?"
"ID","131_susanne_N14@4"
  • Relative pronouns (tagged RPRO) introduce a relative clause, for example, anything that's relevant, an iron hook which he unfastened.
(2.22)
=N(" anything that <apos>s relevant ")
"NP","D","anything"
"NP","IP-REL","NP-SBJ","RPRO","that"
"NP","IP-REL","BEP;~La","<apos>s"
"NP","IP-REL","ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","relevant"
"ID","2#88_christine_T29@2,4"
(2.23)
=N(" an iron hook which he unfastened ")
"NP-SBJ","D","an"
"NP-SBJ","N","iron"
"NP-SBJ","N","hook"
"NP-SBJ","IP-REL","NP-OB1","RPRO","which"
"NP-SBJ","IP-REL","NP-SBJ","PRO","he"
"NP-SBJ","IP-REL","VBD","unfastened"
"ID","28#38_susanne_N12@6,8"
  • Indefinite pronouns with quantification (tagged Q — the same tag used for quantifiers; see section 2.5) come in two broad types: compounds, for example, everyone knows, nobody knows, and words which often occur with the preposition of, such as, all of them, none of them.
(2.24)
=N(" everyone knows ")
"IP-ADV","NP-SBJ","Q","everyone"
"IP-ADV","VBP","knows"
"ID","13#80_susanne_G17@3"
(2.25)

"IP-MAT-OB1","VBP","knows"
"IP-MAT-OB1","CP-QUE-OB1","IP-SUB","NP-1","WPRO","what"
"IP-MAT-OB1","CP-QUE-OB1","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","N","God"
"IP-MAT-OB1","CP-QUE-OB1","IP-SUB","VBP;~Ipr","looks"
"IP-MAT-OB1","CP-QUE-OB1","IP-SUB","PP-PRD2","P-ROLE","like"
"IP-MAT-OB1","CP-QUE-OB1","IP-SUB","PP-PRD2","NP","*ICH*-1"
"ID","15#51_texts_TED_talk_11@5"

"ID","24#90_texts_IBM_1401@2"
(2.27)

"NP-SBJ","Q","none"
"NP-SBJ","PP","P-ROLE","of"
"NP-SBJ","PP","NP","PRO","them"
"ID","9#499_christine_T03@2"
  • Indefinite pronouns not subclassified as Q (tagged D — the same tag used for indefinite determiners; see section 2.5), for example, somebody comes, but also some of them. The indefinite pronoun one, for example, as one would expect, typically occurs in formal contexts. The pronoun you (always tagged PRO) can also be used indefinitely, for example, as you can see.
(2.28)

"PP-SCON-MOD","IP-ADV","MD","can"
"PP-SCON-MOD","IP-ADV","VB","see"
"ID","32#217_texts_TED_talk_11@6"
  • Demonstrative pronouns (tagged D; see Table 2.4 for the full range) indicate a specific referent in terms of the distance (real or metaphorical) from the speaker, for example, I don't want that. I'll have these, Demonstratives are tagged D, since they can also function as definite determiners which modify nouns within a noun phrase, for example, this point.

Table 2.4: Demonstrative pronouns/determiners (D)

Near speaker Away from speaker
Singular this that
Plural these those

2.3   Words that can head an adjective phrase

For a word to be an adjective, it must be able to function as the head of an adjective phrase (ADJP; see section 3.3), often as the only element of the phrase. Adjectives are tagged as in Table 2.5 to distinguish comparative and superlative forms from general forms.

Table 2.5: Tags for words that can head an adjective phrase

ADJGeneral adjective: an adjective not subclassified as ADJR or ADJS (e.g. old, good, male)
ADJRComparative adjective (e.g. older, better)
ADJSSuperlative adjective (e.g. oldest, best)

    The comparative form of an adjective is typically indicated by the suffix {er}, whereas the superlative form is typically indicated by the suffix {est}; see Table 2.6.

Table 2.6: Forms of adjectives

General (ADJ) Comparative (ADJR) Superlative (ADJS)
Gradable old older oldest
good better best
Non-gradable male

2.4   Words that can head an adverb phrase

For a word to be an adverb, it must be able to function as the head of an adverb phrase (ADVP; see section 3.4), often as the only element of the phrase. Adverbs are tagged as in Table 2.7 to distinguish comparative, superlative, and WH froms from general forms.

Table 2.7: Tags for words that can head an adverb phrase

ADVGeneral adverb: an adverb not subclassified as ADVR, ADVS, RADV, or WADV (e.g. often, well, really).
ADVRComparative adverb (e.g. more, less, farther)
ADVSSuperlative adverb (e.g. most, least, farthest)
RADVWh-adverb that is the relative adverb of a relative clause (e.g. how, when, where, whereby)
WADVWh-adverb (e.g. how, when, where, why)
RPAdverbial particle (e.g. up, off, out)

2.5   Other noun phrase level words

Section 2.2 above has already discussed words that can head a noun phrase. Table 2.8 gives tags for other words that can be immediate components of a noun phrase, but that can't be a noun phrase head. Rather, these words precede the head within noun phrases and function to modify the head in terms of definiteness, item under question, or quantity.

Table 2.8: Tags for words that can be immediate components of a noun phrase

DDeterminer, which includes articles (e.g. a, the) and demonstratives (e.g. this, that)
RDWh-determiner that is the relative determiner of a relative clause (e.g. what, whatever)
WDWh-determiner (e.g. which, what, whichever)
NUMNumeral (e.g. one, 1975)
QQuantifier (e.g. every, no)

    Prototypical nouns can take one of two articles:

  • a (/an) or the in the singular,
  • some or the in the plural.

Articles for the singular head noun wish and the plural head noun wishes are seen in (2.35).

(2.35)
(D a) (N wish)                   (D the) (N wish)
(D some) (NS wishes)             (D the) (NS wishes)

2.6   Verbs

Verbs occur at clause levels of structure in the annotation. There are tags to subclassify verbs in accordance to their form:

  • lexical verbs (VBP, VBD, VB, VAG, VVN) (discussed in section 2.6.1),
  • HAVE verbs (HVP, HVD, HV, HAG, HVN) (discussed in section 2.6.2),
  • BE verbs (BEP, BED, BE, BAG, BEN) (discussed in section 2.6.3),
  • DO verbs (DOP, DOD, DO, DAG, DON) (discussed in section 2.6.4), and
  • modal verbs (MD) (discussed in section 2.6.5).

2.6.1   Lexical verbs

Verbs can change in shape to show tense. For example, the verb SUPPORT in John supports Peter takes a third person present tense {s} inflection, while in John supported Peter it has a past tense {ed} inflection. A verb that has tense is called a finite verb.

    Tenseless forms of verbs are called nonfinite verbs, which are comprised of:

  • infinitive forms,
  • present participle forms, and
  • past participle forms.

Note that participle forms are tenseless despite their full names! Infinitive forms occur in infinitive clauses, often preceded by the infinitive marker to (e.g. John happened to support Peter.). Present participles are used in the progressive construction (e.g. John is supporting Peter). Past participles are used in the perfect construction (e.g. John has supported Peter) and the passive construction (e.g. Peter is supported by John).

    The distinctions in verb forms just sketched are captured in the tags for lexical verbs of Table 2.9.

Table 2.9: Tags for lexical verbs

VBPThe present tense form of lexical verbs (e.g. forgets, sends, lives, returns, forget, send, live, return)
VBDThe past tense form of lexical verbs (e.g. forgot, sent, lived, returned)
VBThe infinitive form of lexical verbs (e.g. forget, send, live, return)
VAGThe present participle ({ing}) form of lexical verbs (used in the progressive construction) (e.g. forgetting, sending, living, returning)
VVNThe past participle ({en}) form of lexical verbs (used in the perfect construction and the passive construction) (e.g. forgotten, sent, lived, returned)

    Table 2.10 further illustrates with examples the distinctions between the different lexical verb forms. This includes examples of irregular verbs that do not have a regular past tense inflection.

Table 2.10: Forms of lexical verb

Tensed forms Tenseless forms
Tense Infinitive (VB) Participles
Present (VBP) Past (VBD) Present (VAG) Past (VVN)
3rd person singular Other
Regular reaches reach reached reach reaching reached
supports support supported support supporting supported
Irregular writes write wrote write writing written
sinks sink sank sink sinking sunk
puts put put put putting put

2.6.2   HAVE

The forms of HAVE are tagged as in Table 2.11.

Table 2.11: Tags for HAVE

HVPThe present tense forms of the verb HAVE: have, 've, has, 's
HVDThe past tense form of the verb HAVE: had, 'd
HVThe infinitive form of the verb HAVE: have
HAGThe present participle ({ing}) form of the verb HAVE: having
HVNThe past participle ({en}) form of the verb HAVE: had

HAVE has the non-contracted inflections of Table 2.12.

Table 2.12: Forms of HAVE

Tensed forms Tenseless forms
Tense Infinitive (HV) Participles
Present (HVP) Past (HVD) Present (HAG) Past (HVN)
3rd person singular Other
has have had have having had

2.6.3   BE

The forms of BE are tagged as in Table 2.13.

Table 2.13: Tags for BE

BEPThe present tense forms of the verb BE: i.e. is, am, are, 'm, 're, and 's
BEDThe past tense forms of the verb BE: was and were
BEThe infinitive form of the verb BE: be
BAGThe present participle ({ing}) form of the verb BE: being
BENThe past participle ({en}) form of the verb BE: been

Table 2.14 presents an overview of the eight different non-contracted forms of BE. This is the widest range of distinct forms for the same verb lexme in English, with extra person-number contrasts in the past and present tenses.

Table 2.14: Forms of BE

Tensed forms Tenseless forms
Tense Infinitive (BE) Participles
Present (BEP) Past (BED) Present (BAG) Past (BEN)
3rd person singular 1st person singular Other singular plural
is am are was were be being been

2.6.4   DO

The forms of DO are tagged as in Table 2.15.

Table 2.15: Tags for DO

DOPThe present tense forms of the verb DO: do, does, 's
DODThe past tense form of the verb DO: did
DOThe infinitive form of the verb DO: do
DAGThe present participle ({ing}) form of the verb DO: doing
DONThe past participle ({en}) form of the verb DO: done

Table 2.16: Forms of DO

2.6.5   Modal verbs

Modal verbs express meanings such as certainty, ability, or obligation. The main modal verbs are WILL, WOULD, CAN, COULD, MAY, MIGHT, SHALL, SHOULD, MUST and OUGHT. A modal verb only has finite forms and has no suffixes (e.g. I sing — he sings, but I must — he must). Modal verbs are tagged as in Table 2.17.

Table 2.17: Tag for modal verbs

MDModal verbs (e.g. will, would, can, could, 'll, 'd)

2.7   Other clause level words

Besides verbs, other clause level components are words with the tags of Table 2.18.

Table 2.18: Tags for clause level components

EXExistential there, i.e. there occurring in the there is ... or there are ... construction
NEGThe negative particle not or n't
TOInfinitive marker to

    We can see these clause level components in the annotation of (2.36). This begins with there (EX) to create an existential construction, and includes the negation particle n't (NEG). The subject of the existential construction (NP-ESBJ) has an infinitive relative clause (IP-INF-REL) that includes infinitive marker to (TO).

(2.36)
=N(" There were n<apos>t any incidents to get in the way . ")
"IP-MAT","EX","There"
"IP-MAT","BED;~cat_t","were"
"IP-MAT","NEG","n<apos>t"
"IP-MAT","NP-ESBJ","D","any"
"IP-MAT","NP-ESBJ","NS","incidents"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-CAT","TO","to"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-CAT","VB;~Ipr","get"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-CAT","PP-LOC","P-ROLE","in"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-CAT","PP-LOC","NP","D","the"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-CAT","PP-LOC","NP","N","way"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","143_bnc_C08@2,6,14"

    It is also possible for a word tagged RP to occur as a clause level component. The RP tag is used to mark adverbial particles (e.g. up, off, out) and was seen in section 2.4 as the tag for a word that can head an adverb phrase. When an RP tagged word occurs as a clause level component it is part of a phrasal verb, as in (2.37).

(2.37)
=N(" hold on a second ")
"IP-MAT","VBP;~phr_Vp","hold"
"IP-MAT","RP","on"
"IP-MAT","NP-TMP","D","a"
"IP-MAT","NP-TMP","N","second"
"ID","33_christine_T28@4"

2.8   Connective words

So far we have considered words that serve as components of either phrases or clauses. There is a further class of words with the tags of Table 2.19 that serve as the means to connect phrases and clauses.

Table 2.19: Tags for connective words

CONJCoordinating conjunction (e.g. and, or, but)
CThe complementizer that
WQMarker of indirect question (whether or if)
P-CONNSubordinating conjunction (e.g. although, when)
P-ROLERole preposition (e.g. in, of, under)

When phrases and clauses are connected they are said to be COMPLEX.


2.9   Punctuation

Punctuation points, quotation marks, and brackets (‘.’ ‘?’ ‘!’ ‘:’ ‘;’ ‘,’ ‘-’ ‘(’ ‘)’ etc.) are treated as words for the purposes of word tagging with the tags of Table 2.20.

Table 2.20: Tags for punctuation

PUPunctuation: general separating mark — i.e. . , ! : ; - or ?
PULPunctuation: left bracket — i.e. ( or [
PUQPunctuation: quotation mark — i.e. ' or "
PURPunctuation: right bracket — i.e. ) or ]

This makes punctuation part of a sentence in its own right. When creating constituent structure, punctuation is placed as high as possible. For example, a full stop that ends a sentence is treated as the last constituent of the highest clause layer (IP/CP/FRAG).


2.10   Interjection, reaction signals, and formulaic expressions

Interjections, reaction signals, and formulaic expressions are treated as single words with the tags of Table 2.21. They are placed high in constituent structure, occurring typically as clause or fragment layer components.

Table 2.21: Tags for interjection, reaction signals, and formulaic expressions

INTJInterjection (e.g. aah, eh, ummmmm)
REACTReaction signal (e.g. good_grief, really, yes, wow)
FRMFormulaic expression (e.g. good_afternoon, you_see, thank_you)
(2.38)
=N(" Well <comma> no <comma> of course not . ")
"FRAG","INTJ","Well"
"FRAG","PU","<comma>"
"FRAG","REACT","no"
"FRAG","PU","<comma>"
"FRAG","ADVP-MOD","ADV","of_course"
"FRAG","NEG","not"
"FRAG","PU","."
"ID","19_bnc_B19@2,6"
(2.39)
=N(" Thank you very much . ")
"FRAG","FRM","Thank_you"
"FRAG","NP-MNR","ADJP","ADVP","ADV","very"
"FRAG","NP-MNR","ADJP","ADJ","much"
"FRAG","PU","."
"ID","43_texts_TED_talk_11@2"

2.11   Other tags

Tags for other possible elements of a parse are given in Table 2.22.

Table 2.22: Other tags

FOFormula

Chapter 3
Phrases


3.1   Introduction

The phrase is an intermediate level of organisation between a word and the clause. Phrases can be characterised partly by their external function and partly by their internal form. Table 3.1 gives an overview of typical external functions for the phrases discussed in this chapter.

Table 3.1: Typical external functions for phrases

External functions phrase
noun adjective adverb preposition
clause level selected subject (-SBJ) ☆
object primary (-OB1) ☆
secondary (-OB2) ☆
predicative (-PRD(2)) ☆ ☆
adverbial ☆ ☆ ☆
genitive (-GENV) ☆
vocative (-VOC) ☆

    By “form” is meant the way the structure of the phrase is made up of other, smaller elements. Each phrase is made up of a HEAD word and any DEPENDENTs. The head word may be the only element of the phrase. A phrase is named after the word class of the head word.


3.2   Noun phrase (NP)

This section considers the internal form and external functions of noun phrases.

Table 3.2: Tag for bare noun phrase

NPnoun phrase

3.2.1   NP heads and ellipsis

A constituent is STRUCTURALLY COMPLETE when not reduced by ellipsis. ELLIPSIS is the ommission of one or more elements that can be recovered or understood from the linguistic or situational context. All structurally complete noun phrases have a head word. Section 2.2 gives the list of tags for elements that can head noun phrases, including proper nouns and pronouns.

    As an example of noun phrase head ellipsis, consider (3.1), with its predicative noun phrase (NP-PRD2) lacking a head noun. In a context discussing siblings, Alan is understood to be the youngest sibling.

(3.1)
=N(" Alan is the youngest . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{ALAN}","NPR","Alan"
"IP-MAT","BEP;~Ln","is"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","D","the"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","ADJP","ADJS","youngest"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","2_age_09_M23@7"

While there is no additional marking to indicate the noun ellipsis in the annotation of (3.1), the overall structure gives this information: There is a noun phrase projection that does not contain a head word.

3.2.2   NP dependents

A structurally complete noun phrase can consist of one head word (e.g. the pronoun we or the noun pizza), or it can consist of a head word with a number of dependents. Dependents modify the head word by adding information.

    Possible dependents include: determiners (e.g. the pizza), adjective phrases (e.g. healthy pizza), nominal modifiers (nouns which modify other nouns, e.g. vegetable pizza), and relative clauses (e.g. pizza that I made). It is possible to have multiple dependents. For example, (3.2) is a noun phrase containing determiner the, adjective phrase healthy, nominal modifier vegetable, head noun pizza, and relative clause that I made.

(3.2)
=N(" The healthy vegetable pizza that I made ")
"NP-SBJ","D","The"
"NP-SBJ","ADJP","ADJ","healthy"
"NP-SBJ","N","vegetable"
"NP-SBJ","N","pizza"
"NP-SBJ","IP-REL","NP-OB1","RPRO","that"
"NP-SBJ","IP-REL","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
"NP-SBJ","IP-REL","VBD;~Tn","made"
"ID","2#21_samples_purple_basic@9,2,4,7,11"

    With both nouns (e.g. vegetable) and adjective phrases (e.g. healthy) able to act as modifiers in a noun phrase, other criteria is needed to distinguish nouns functioning as nominal modifiers from single word adjective phrases. For example, adjectives do not take number marking, while vegetable can be made plural (vegetables).

    A dependent in the NP can also be categorised according to its position in the NP:

  • Determiners (D), quantifiers (Q), genitive noun phrases (NP-GENV), numerals (NUM), adjective phrases (ADJP), nominal modifiers (N or NS), and some adverb phrases (ADVP), occur before the NP head (e.g., about the first week day in June, the young printer's apprentice, the computer skills course, all the really obvious things).
(3.3)
=N(" about the first week in June ")
"NP-FOC","ADVP","ADV","about"
"NP-FOC","D","the"
"NP-FOC","NUM","first"
"NP-FOC","N","week"
"NP-FOC","PP","P-ROLE","in"
"NP-FOC","PP","NP","NPR","June"
"ID","7#2_age_11_H29@9,2,5,7"
(3.4)
=N(" The young printer <apos>s apprentice ")
"NP-SBJ","D","The"
"NP-SBJ","ADJP","ADJ","young"
"NP-SBJ","NP-GENV","N","printer"
"NP-SBJ","NP-GENV","GENM","<apos>s"
"NP-SBJ","N","apprentice"
"ID","2#16_susanne_G07@12,2,4,7"
(3.5)
=N(" the computer skills course ")
"NP-SBJ","D","the"
"NP-SBJ","N","computer"
"NP-SBJ","NS","skills"
"NP-SBJ","N","course"
"ID","7#10_fce_0101_2001_06@8,2,4,6"
(3.6)
=N(" all the really obvious things ")
"NP-PRD2","Q","all"
"NP-PRD2","D","the"
"NP-PRD2","ADJP","ADVP","ADV","really"
"NP-PRD2","ADJP","ADJ","obvious"
"NP-PRD2","NS","things"
"ID","27#136_christine_T31@12,2,4,6"
  • Single word non-finite clauses (IP-PPL) can occur before the NP head (e.g. a murmuring voice, validated data).
(3.7)
=N(" a murmuring voice ")
"NP","D","a"
"NP","IP-PPL","VAG;~I","murmuring"
"NP","N","voice"
"ID","21#60_classics_JOYCE_1914@7,4"
(3.8)
=N(" validated data ")
"NP-OB1","IP-PPL","NP-LGS","*"
"NP-OB1","IP-PPL","VVN;~Tn","validated"
"NP-OB1","N","data"
"ID","18#92_bnc_B35@7,2"
  • There are restrictions on the order of multiple pre-head constituents. For example, determiners (D) occur before adjective phrases (ADJP), e.g. some testable factual basis.
(3.9)
=N(" some testable factual basis ")
"NP","D","some"
"NP","ADJP;@2","ADJ","testable"
"NP","ADJP;@3","ADJ","factual"
"NP","N","basis"
"ID","21#30_bnc_B26@10,2,4,7"
  • Post-head modifiers in an NP include preposition phrases (PP; e.g. examples of good practise), relative clauses (IP-REL; e.g. pizza that I made), infinitive relative clauses (IP-INF-REL; e.g., more stuff to put in), non-finite clauses (IP-PPL; e.g. a business operator handling personal information), and empty operator clauses (IP-EOP; e.g. a natural resource , free for the taking).
(3.10)
=N(" examples of good practice ")
"NP","NS","examples"
"NP","PP","P-ROLE","of"
"NP","PP","NP","ADJP","ADJ","good"
"NP","PP","NP","N","practice"
"ID","39#23_bnc_B21@2,4"
(3.11)
=N(" more stuff to put in ")
"NP","ADJP","ADJR","more"
"NP","N","stuff"
"NP","IP-INF-REL","NP-OB1","*T*"
"NP","IP-INF-REL","TO","to"
"NP","IP-INF-REL","VB;~Tn.p","put"
"NP","IP-INF-REL","ADVP-DIR","RP","in"
"ID","2#186_christine_T16@5,7"
(3.12)
=N(" a business operator handling personal information ")
"NP","D","a"
"NP","N","business"
"NP","N","operator"
"NP","IP-PPL","VAG;~Tn","handling"
"NP","IP-PPL","NP-OB1","ADJP","ADJ","personal"
"NP","IP-PPL","NP-OB1","N","information"
"ID","17#12_texts_law_h15A119@6,8"
(3.13)
=N(" a natural resource <comma> free for the taking ")
"NP","D","a"
"NP","ADJP","ADJ","natural"
"NP","N","resource"
"NP","PU","<comma>"
"NP","IP-EOP","NP-SBJ","*T*"
"NP","IP-EOP","ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","free"
"NP","IP-EOP","PP-CNT","P-ROLE","for"
"NP","IP-EOP","PP-CNT","NP","D","the"
"NP","IP-EOP","PP-CNT","NP","N","taking"
"ID","30#97_susanne_N11@7,11"
  • Some nouns select a post-head dependent that can be a single CP-THT clause (e.g., such a way that specific personal information can be easily retrieved), or a single IP-INF clause (e.g., My wish to meet Samuel Beckett)
(3.14)
=N(" such a way that specific personal information can be easily retrieved ")
"NP","ADJP","ADJ","such"
"NP","D","a"
"NP","N","way"
"NP","CP-THT","IP-SUB","C","that"
"NP","CP-THT","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","ADJP;@1","ADJ","specific"
"NP","CP-THT","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","ADJP;@2","ADJ","personal"
"NP","CP-THT","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","N","information"
"NP","CP-THT","IP-SUB","MD","can"
"NP","CP-THT","IP-SUB","BE;~cat_e","be"
"NP","CP-THT","IP-SUB","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-LGS","*"
"NP","CP-THT","IP-SUB","IP-PPL-CAT","ADVP-MNR","ADV","easily"
"NP","CP-THT","IP-SUB","IP-PPL-CAT","VVN;~Tn","retrieved"
"ID","84#11_texts_law_h15A119@7,9"
(3.15)
=N(" My wish to meet Samuel Beckett ")
"NP-SBJ","NP-GENV","PRO","My"
"NP-SBJ","N","wish"
"NP-SBJ","IP-INF","TO","to"
"NP-SBJ","IP-INF","VB","meet"
"NP-SBJ","IP-INF","NP-OB1","NPR","Samuel"
"NP-SBJ","IP-INF","NP-OB1","NPR","Beckett"
"ID","2#4_susanne_G12@5,7"
  • Some adjective phrases (often with their own dependents) can occur as post-head modifiers (e.g. something unusual, capital so vital for growth).
(3.16)
=N(" something unusual ")
"NP-SBJ","D","something"
"NP-SBJ","ADJP","ADJ","unusual"
"ID","5#59_bnc_B17@2,4"

"NP-OB1","ADJP","PP","P-ROLE","for"
"NP-OB1","ADJP","PP","NP","N","growth"
"ID","44#62_susanne_G22@2,4"

(3.18)

3.2.3   Nominal intermediate level

When there is need for intermediate noun phrase structure in the annotation, NML is used.

Table 3.3: Tag for nominal intermediate level

NMLnominal intermediate level

This occurs when either:

(i)
a nominal modifier is itself modified, or
(ii)
there is coordination within the noun phrase.

Scenarios for (ii) are illustrated in section 3.2.4. A scenario for (i) is illustrated by (3.19), with its adjective modifier secondary of the nominal modifier school.

(3.19)
=N(" Total secondary school spending in Britain per pupil ")
"NP-SBJ","ADJP","ADJ","Total"
"NP-SBJ","NML","ADJP","ADJ","secondary"
"NP-SBJ","NML","N","school"
"NP-SBJ","N","spending"
"NP-SBJ","PP;@4","P-ROLE","in"
"NP-SBJ","PP;@4","NP","NPR","Britain"
"NP-SBJ","PP;@5","P-ROLE","per"
"NP-SBJ","PP;@5","NP","N","pupil"
"ID","2#107_bnc_B41@5"

Another example of (i) is (3.20), with its preposition phrase modifier in aid of the nominal modifier grant.

(3.20)
=N(" a grant in aid program ")
"NP","D","a"
"NP","NML","N","grant"
"NP","NML","PP","P-ROLE","in"
"NP","NML","PP","NP","N","aid"
"NP","N","program"
"ID","73#66_susanne_A08@4"

This use of NML can be recursive:

(3.21)
=N(" the 2 <hyphen> score <hyphen> year milestone ")
"NP","D","the"
"NP","NML","NML","NUM","2"
"NP","NML","NML","PU","<hyphen>"
"NP","NML","NML","N","score"
"NP","NML","PU","<hyphen>"
"NP","NML","N","year"
"NP","N","milestone"
"ID","47#62_susanne_A13@4,5"
(3.22)
=N(" middle <mdash> and upper <hyphen> level management personnel ")
"NP-OB1","NML","NML","ADJP","AML","ADJP","ADJ","middle"
"NP-OB1","NML","NML","ADJP","AML","PU","<mdash>"
"NP-OB1","NML","NML","ADJP","AML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"NP-OB1","NML","NML","ADJP","AML","CONJP","ADJP","ADJ","upper"
"NP-OB1","NML","NML","PU","<hyphen>"
"NP-OB1","NML","NML","N","level"
"NP-OB1","NML","N","management"
"NP-OB1","N","personnel"
"ID","14#56_susanne_J22@2,3"

Further scenarios for (i) are (3.23) and (3.24). These illustrate how an NML layer can be used to isolate noun phrase content for modification by a quantifier.

(3.23)
=N(" one in every five calls ")
"NP-PRD2","NUM","one"
"NP-PRD2","PP","P-ROLE","in"
"NP-PRD2","PP","NP","Q","every"
"NP-PRD2","PP","NP","NML","NUM","five"
"NP-PRD2","PP","NP","NML","NS","calls"
"ID","20#27_texts_Duplex@10"
(3.24)
=N(" each one ")
"NP","Q","each"
"NP","NML","D","one"
"ID","30#2_texts_freedoom@4"

3.2.4   NP conjunction

When the content of a noun phrase involves conjunction, all conjuncts, except the first, receive a CONJP projection. These CONJP projections are adjoined to the first conjunct under an NML layer.

Table 3.4: Tag for conjunction phrase

CONJPconjunction phrase

CONJP layers exist irrespective of the presence of an overt CONJ head, as illustrated in (3.29).

(3.29)
=N(" a kitchen <comma> a hall <comma> a dining room <comma> and a living room or sitting room ")
"NP","NML","NP","D","a"
"NP","NML","NP","N","kitchen"
"NP","NML","PU","<comma>"
"NP","NML","CONJP;@3","NP","D","a"
"NP","NML","CONJP;@3","NP","N","hall"
"NP","NML","PU","<comma>"
"NP","NML","CONJP;@5","NP","D","a"
"NP","NML","CONJP;@5","NP","N","dining"
"NP","NML","CONJP;@5","NP","N","room"
"NP","NML","PU","<comma>"
"NP","NML","CONJP;@7","CONJ","and"
"NP","NML","CONJP;@7","NP","D","a"
"NP","NML","CONJP;@7","NP","NML","NP","N","living"
"NP","NML","CONJP;@7","NP","NML","NP","N","room"
"NP","NML","CONJP;@7","NP","NML","CONJP","CONJ","or"
"NP","NML","CONJP;@7","NP","NML","CONJP","NP","N","sitting"
"NP","NML","CONJP;@7","NP","NML","CONJP","NP","N","room"
"ID","57#8_age_09_M59@2,10,18,28"

Example (3.29) also shows how conjuncts can themselves contain conjuncts. Such LAYERED conjunction is easiest to identify when different coordinators are used at each level, as with and and or in (3.29). Placement choices with layered conjunction will have consequences for the meaning captured by the annotation when different coordinators are involved.

    If there is a CONJ that starts the sequence of conjunctions, then this initial CONJ is placed at the NML layer, as illustrated in (3.30).

(3.30)
=N(" not only his own life <comma> but Geoffrey <apos>s as well ")
"NP-OB1","NML","NEG","not"
"NP-OB1","NML","ADVP-MOD;@2","ADV","only"
"NP-OB1","NML","NP","NP-GENV;{ALASTAIR}","PRO","his"
"NP-OB1","NML","NP","ADJP","ADJ","own"
"NP-OB1","NML","NP","N","life"
"NP-OB1","NML","PU","<comma>"
"NP-OB1","NML","CONJP","CONJ","but"
"NP-OB1","NML","CONJP","NP","NP-GENV;{GEOFFREY}","NPR","Geoffrey"
"NP-OB1","NML","CONJP","NP","NP-GENV;{GEOFFREY}","GENM","<apos>s"
"NP-OB1","NML","ADVP-MOD;@6","ADV","as_well"
"ID","22#3_lob_N01@3,17"

Example (3.30) also shows how an ADVP-MOD element can occur at the end of a conjunction sequence, in which case it occurs at the level of the prior conjuncts. An ADVP-MOD or ADVP-CNT can also occur before a conjunct, in which case it occurs at the CONJP layer of the conjunct it precedes, as illustrated in (3.31) and (3.32),

(3.31)
=N(" The volume and <comma> perhaps <comma> even the characteristics of bronchial arterial blood flow ")
"NP-SBJ","NML","NP","D","The"
"NP-SBJ","NML","NP","N","volume"
"NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","PU","<comma>"
"NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","ADVP-MOD;@3","ADV","perhaps"
"NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","PU","<comma>"
"NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","ADVP-MOD;@5","ADV","even"
"NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","NP","D","the"
"NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","NP","NS","characteristics"
"NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","P-ROLE","of"
"NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","NP","ADJP;@1","ADJ","bronchial"
"NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","NP","ADJP;@2","ADJ","arterial"
"NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","NP","N","blood"
"NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","NP","N","flow"
"ID","2#34_susanne_J12@8,13,18"
(3.32)
=N(" a more severe problem of flooding and <comma> consequently <comma> environmental degradation through soil erosion and deforestation ")
"NP","NML","NP","D","a"
"NP","NML","NP","ADJP","ADVP","ADVR","more"
"NP","NML","NP","ADJP","ADJ","severe"
"NP","NML","NP","N","problem"
"NP","NML","NP","PP","P-ROLE","of"
"NP","NML","NP","PP","IP-PPL","VAG","flooding"
"NP","NML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"NP","NML","CONJP","PU","<comma>"
"NP","NML","CONJP","ADVP-CNT","ADV","consequently"
"NP","NML","CONJP","PU","<comma>"
"NP","NML","CONJP","NP","ADJP","ADJ","environmental"
"NP","NML","CONJP","NP","N","degradation"
"NP","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","P-ROLE","through"
"NP","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","NP","NML","NP","N","soil"
"NP","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","NP","NML","NP","N","erosion"
"NP","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","NP","NML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"NP","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","NP","NML","CONJP","NP","N","deforestation"
"ID","75#6_young_adult_E93@20,25"

    In the cases of NML with coordination seen so far the NML itself forms the head of the overall noun phrase that contains it, with nothing else outside this complex head. It is also possible for a complex head involving coordination to itself be modified, as (3.33) demonstrates.

(3.33)
=N(" the environmental issues and problems that face us today ")
"NP","D","the"
"NP","ADJP","ADJ","environmental"
"NP","NML","NP","NS","issues"
"NP","NML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"NP","NML","CONJP","NP","NS","problems"
"NP","IP-REL","NP-SBJ","RPRO","that"
"NP","IP-REL","VBP","face"
"NP","IP-REL","NP-OB1","PRO","us"
"NP","IP-REL","ADVP-TMP","ADV","today"
"ID","11#1_young_adult_E13@7"

    The modifier of a head noun can itself involve noun phrase coordination internally, as (3.34) and (3.36) illustrate. In such cases there is an NML layer to introduce the modifier that immediately contains as its only daughter an NML layer that contains the coordination.

(3.34)
=N(" a mouse and keyboard combination ")
"NP-SBJ","D","a"
"NP-SBJ","NML","NML","NP","N","mouse"
"NP-SBJ","NML","NML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"NP-SBJ","NML","NML","CONJP","NP","N","keyboard"
"NP-SBJ","N","combination"
"ID","13#22_texts_freedoom@4,5"
(3.35)
=N(" about    number two or three or something like that ")
"NP","NML","NP","ADVP","ADV","about"
"NP","NML","NP","N","number"
"NP","NML","NP","NML","NML","NP","NUM","two"
"NP","NML","NP","NML","NML","CONJP","CONJ","or"
"NP","NML","NP","NML","NML","CONJP","NP","NUM","three"
"NP","NML","CONJP","CONJ","or"
"NP","NML","CONJP","NP","D","something"
"NP","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","P-ROLE","like"
"NP","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","NP","D","that"
"ID","25#365_christine_T08@7,8"
(3.36)
=N(" Even more complex and obviously cortically induced forms of emotional arousal ")
"NP-SBJ","NML","NML","NP","ADJP","ADVP","ADVP","ADV","Even"
"NP-SBJ","NML","NML","NP","ADJP","ADVP","ADVR","more"
"NP-SBJ","NML","NML","NP","ADJP","ADJ","complex"
"NP-SBJ","NML","NML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"NP-SBJ","NML","NML","CONJP","NP","IP-PPL","NP-LGS","*"
"NP-SBJ","NML","NML","CONJP","NP","IP-PPL","ADVP-MOD","ADV","obviously"
"NP-SBJ","NML","NML","CONJP","NP","IP-PPL","ADVP-MNR","ADV","cortically"
"NP-SBJ","NML","NML","CONJP","NP","IP-PPL","VVN","induced"
"NP-SBJ","NS","forms"
"NP-SBJ","PP","P-ROLE","of"
"NP-SBJ","PP","NP","ADJP","ADJ","emotional"
"NP-SBJ","PP","NP","N","arousal"
"ID","2#4_susanne_J17@2,3"

    Note how the conjuncts of the internal noun phrase coordination that is a head noun modifier in (3.36) actually contain respectively: only (i) an adjective phrase, and (ii) a participial clause. The level of noun phrase for these conjuncts is motivated by the need for all conjuncts of a conjunction to be phrases of the same type.

(3.37)
=N(" its coverage <mdash> fiction <comma> biography <comma> travel <comma> hobbies <comma> popular science <comma> etc. ")

3.2.6   NP functions

The notion of function helps to establish how a given NP acts as a single unit (constituent) regardless of size. Thus, the large (3.2) above can be seen as a subject (NP-SBJ) in (3.39) or object (NP-OB1) in (3.40) in a similar way to a NP with fewer elements, e.g, a head noun with definite article, or a pronoun as the single phrase element.

(3.39)
a.
(NP-SBJ The healthy vegetable pizza that I made) was delicious .
b.
(NP-SBJ The pizza) was delicious .
c.
(NP-SBJ It) was delicious .
(3.40)
a.
They loved (NP-OB1 the healthy vegetable pizza that I made) .
b.
They loved (NP-OB1 the pizza) .
c.
They loved (NP-OB1 it) .

    The examples of (3.41) show that the same noun phrase (that way) is able to take on various different functions depending on its contribution to the containing clause.

(3.41)
a.
(NP-SBJ That way) is completely wrong . [subject]
b.
Someone will discover (NP-OB1 that way) . [primary object]
c.
They will keep it (NP-PRD that way) . [predicative]
d.
He hurried (NP-DIR that way) . [directional]
e.
Just hold it (NP-MNR that way) . [manner]

    Section 4.3.4 lists node label extensions that indicate adverbial functions, all of which except -BNF and -COM can be used with NP. An NP can also take an adverbial function from occuring as complement of a preposition (such as of, to, with, at, by, etc.) (see section 3.5.4).

    An NP might also occur in the genitive construction, seen in section 2.2.5, with the tag of Table 3.5.

Table 3.5: Tag for genitive noun phrase

NP-GENVgenitive noun phrase

    Finally, an NP might be a vocative with the tag of Table 3.6, as in (3.42).

Table 3.6: Tag for vocative noun phrase

NP-VOCvocative noun phrase
(3.42)
=N(" <ldquo> One thing <comma> Summers <rdquo> <comma> Brenner said . ")
"IP-MAT","PUQ","<ldquo>"
"IP-MAT","FRAG-OB1","NP","NUM","One"
"IP-MAT","FRAG-OB1","NP","N","thing"
"IP-MAT","FRAG-OB1","PU","<comma>"
"IP-MAT","FRAG-OB1","NP-VOC","NPR","Summers"
"IP-MAT","PUQ","<rdquo>"
"IP-MAT","PU","<comma>"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","NPR","Brenner"
"IP-MAT","VBD","said"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","12_susanne_N12@4"

See 4.2.3 for discussion on NP-VOC in imperative sentences.


3.3   Adjective phrase (ADJP)

This section considers the form and function of adjective phrases.

Table 3.7: Tag for bare adjective phrase

ADJPadjective phrase

3.3.1   ADJP heads

The head of an ADJP is an adjective. The head adjective can be:

  • plain (ADJ; e.g. big),
  • comparative (ADJR; e.g. bigger), or
  • superlative (ADJS; e.g. biggest).

3.3.2   ADJP dependents

Instances of ADJP can have various dependents which modify the head, such as adverb phrases (ADVP), preposition phrases (PP), or clauses.

  • Adverbs modify the head to provide information about degree and commonly occur before the head (e.g. so important), although a few occur after (e.g. easy enough).
(3.43)
=N(" so important ")
"ADJP-PRD2","ADVP","ADV","so"
"ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","important"
"ID","34#4_texts_ANC_110CYL067@5,2"
(3.44)
=N(" easy enough ")
"ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","easy"
"ADJP-PRD2","ADVP","ADV","enough"
"ID","7#152_classics_WILDE_1_1888@2,4"
  • Some adverbs as premodifiers, especially very and really, can be repeated (e.g. very very rude).
(3.45)
=N(" very very rude ")
"ADJP","ADVP;@1","ADV","very"
"ADJP","ADVP;@2","ADV","very"
"ADJP","ADJ","rude"
"ID","16#455_christine_T21@8,2,5"
  • An NP can also act as a modifier in an ADJP (e.g. a bit more, aged sixty five years). Such an NP takes no function marking itself.
(3.46)
=N(" a bit more ")
"ADJP","NP","Q","a_bit"
"ADJP","ADJR","more"
"ID","21#114_christine_T25@5,2"
(3.47)
=N(" aged sixty-five years ")
"ADJP","ADJ","aged"
"ADJP","NP","NUM","sixty-five"
"ADJP","NP","NS","years"
"ID","65#70_classics_JOYCE_1914@2,4"
  • Dependents which typically occur after the head of an ADJP are PP (e.g. free from difficulty) and clauses (e.g. able to do that, unaware that certain information actually exists).
(3.48)
=N(" free from difficulty ")
"ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","free"
"ADJP-PRD2","PP","P-ROLE","from"
"ADJP-PRD2","PP","NP","N","difficulty"
"ID","13#56_bnc_B28@2,4"
(3.49)
=N(" able to do that ")
"ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","able"
"ADJP-PRD2","IP-INF","TO","to"
"ADJP-PRD2","IP-INF","DO","do"
"ADJP-PRD2","IP-INF","NP-OB1","D","that"
"ID","12#53_bnc_B17@2,4"
(3.50)
=N(" unaware that certain information actually exists ")
"ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","unaware"
"ADJP-PRD2","CP-THT","IP-SUB","C","that"
"ADJP-PRD2","CP-THT","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","ADJP","ADJ","certain"
"ADJP-PRD2","CP-THT","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","N","information"
"ADJP-PRD2","CP-THT","IP-SUB","ADVP-MOD","ADV","actually"
"ADJP-PRD2","CP-THT","IP-SUB","VBP","exists"
"ID","87#91_bnc_B35@2,4"
  • PP can also act as premodifiers in an ADJP (e.g. for the most part unproductive).
(3.51)
=N(" for the most part unproductive ")
"ADJP","PP","P-ROLE","for"
"ADJP","PP","NP","D","the"
"ADJP","PP","NP","ADVP","ADVS","most"
"ADJP","PP","NP","N","part"
"ADJP","ADJ","unproductive"
"ID","27#52_susanne_G13@13,2"

3.3.3   ADJP conjunction

When the content of an adjective phrase involves conjunction, all conjuncts, except the first, receive a CONJP projection. This gives structure parallel to the conjoined NP structure of section 3.2.4, but with CONJP projections adjoined to the first conjunct under an AML layer. CONJP layers exist irrespective of the presence of an onvert CONJ head, as in (3.52).

(3.52)
=N(" very truculent <comma> grey and massive <comma> with black cavernous nostrils ")
"ADJP-PRD2","ADVP","ADV","very"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","ADJP","ADJ","truculent"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","PU","<comma>"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","CONJP;@3","ADJP","ADJ","grey"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","CONJP;@4","CONJ","and"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","CONJP;@4","ADJP","ADJ","massive"
"ADJP-PRD2","PU","<comma>"
"ADJP-PRD2","PP","P-ROLE","with"
"ADJP-PRD2","PP","NP","ADJP;@1","ADJ","black"
"ADJP-PRD2","PP","NP","ADJP;@2","ADJ","cavernous"
"ADJP-PRD2","PP","NP","NS","nostrils"
"ID","12#108_classics_JOYCE_1914@5,11,15"
(3.53)
=N(" all well and good but a bit clunky and horrible ")
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","ADJP","NP","Q","all"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","ADJP","AML","ADJP","ADJ","well"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","ADJP","AML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","ADJP","AML","CONJP","ADJP","ADJ","good"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","CONJP","CONJ","but"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","CONJP","ADJP","NP","Q","a_bit"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","CONJP","ADJP","AML","ADJP","ADJ","clunky"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","CONJP","ADJP","AML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","CONJP","ADJP","AML","CONJP","ADJP","ADJ","horrible"
"ID","66#63_texts_PaulFellows@2,3,21"
(3.54)
=N(" his craggy yet handsome face ")
"NP","NP-GENV","PRO","his"
"NP","ADJP","AML","ADJP","ADJ","craggy"
"NP","ADJP","AML","CONJP","ADVP-CNT","ADV","yet"
"NP","ADJP","AML","CONJP","ADJP","ADJ","handsome"
"NP","N","face"
"ID","36#109_bnc_C08@11"

Note that coordination where one of the conjuncts is a PP is possible:

(3.55)
=N(" wide and in the dirt ")
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","ADJP","ADJ","wide"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","CONJP","PP","P-ROLE","in"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","CONJP","PP","NP","D","the"
"ADJP-PRD2","AML","CONJP","PP","NP","N","dirt"
"ID","32#10_susanne_A11@2,3,9"
(3.56)
=N(" more and more violent films ")
"NP-SBJ","ADJP","NP","ADJP","AML","ADJP","ADJR","more"
"NP-SBJ","ADJP","NP","ADJP","AML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"NP-SBJ","ADJP","NP","ADJP","AML","CONJP","ADJP","ADJR","more"
"NP-SBJ","ADJP","ADJ","violent"
"NP-SBJ","NS","films"
"ID","52#2_young_adult_E16@4,2"

3.3.4   ADJP functions

ADJP can act as modifiers of a noun phrase head. In doing so, they are said to have ATTRIBUTIVE function. With the annotation, an attributive ADJP occurs without a tag extension, as in (3.57) and (3.58), since placement under NP unambiguously captures the attributive function.

(3.57)
=N(" a good swimmer ")
"NP-PRD2","D","a"
"NP-PRD2","ADJP","ADJ","good"
"NP-PRD2","N","swimmer"
"ID","27#1_age_12_F32@4"
(3.58)
=N(" a very good swimmer ")
"NP-PRD2","D","a"
"NP-PRD2","ADJP","ADVP","ADV","very"
"NP-PRD2","ADJP","ADJ","good"
"NP-PRD2","N","swimmer"
"ID","9#9_age_10_K11@4"

    In a clause, an ADJP will typically have a PREDICATIVE function, marked with the tag extension -PRD(2) (see section 4.3.3), and with a PREDICATE POSITION after verbs that include BE, APPEAR, SEEM, SOUND, MAKE (e.g. The cookbook is practical, This description sounds familiar, It makes me uneasy).


3.4   Adverb phrase (ADVP)

This section considers the form and function of adverb phrases.

Table 3.8: Tag for bare adverb phrase

ADVPadverb phrase

3.4.1   ADVP heads

The head of an ADVP can be:

  • a plain adverb (ADV; e.g. far),
  • a comparative adverb (ADVR; e.g. farther),
  • a superlative adverb (ADVS; e.g. farthest),
  • a Wh-adverb that is the relative adverb of a relative clause (RADV; e.g. how, when, where, whereby),
  • a Wh-adverb (WADV; e.g. how, when, where, why), or
  • an adverb particle (RP; e.g. up).

3.4.2   ADVP dependents

The structure of an ADVP is the same as that of an ADJP.

3.4.3   ADVP conjunction

Conjunction with an ADVP is the same as with an ADJP. With the annotation, CONJP projections are adjoined to the first CONJP-less conjunct under an AML layer.

3.4.4   ADVP functions

Adverb phrases are able to modify adjectives (e.g. totally disgusting), and verbs (e.g. strongly supported). Adverb phrases can also modify preposition phrases (e.g. far up the valley), noun phrases (e.g. nearly a decade), adverb phrases (e.g. Quite often), and clauses (e.g. of_course you can).

(3.59)
=N(" totally disgusting ")
"ADJP-PRD","ADVP","ADV","totally"
"ADJP-PRD","ADJ","disgusting"
"ID","14#89_christine_T19@2"
(3.60)
=N(" strongly supported ")
"IP-PPL","NP-LGS","*"
"IP-PPL","ADVP-MNR","ADV","strongly"
"IP-PPL","VVN;~Tn","supported"
"ID","64#71_susanne_N11@4"
(3.61)
=N(" Far up the valley ")
"PP-LOC","ADVP-DIR","ADV","Far"
"PP-LOC","P-ROLE","up"
"PP-LOC","NP","D","the"
"PP-LOC","NP","N","valley"
"ID","2#116_susanne_N04@2"
(3.62)
=N(" purely and simply for the money ")
"PP-CNT","ADVP-MOD","AML","ADVP","ADV","purely"
"PP-CNT","ADVP-MOD","AML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"PP-CNT","ADVP-MOD","AML","CONJP","ADVP","ADV","simply"
"PP-CNT","P-ROLE","for"
"PP-CNT","NP","D","the"
"PP-CNT","NP","N","money"
"ID","81#491_christine_T29@2"
(3.63)
=N(" nearly a decade ")
"NP","ADVP","ADV","nearly"
"NP","D","a"
"NP","N","decade"
"ID","28#81_bnc_B37@2"
(3.64)
=N(" Quite often ")
"ADVP-TMP","ADVP","ADV","Quite"
"ADVP-TMP","ADV","often"
"ID","2#18_susanne_J10@2"
(3.65)
=N(" of course you can ")
"IP-MAT","ADVP-MOD","ADV","of_course"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","you"
"IP-MAT","MD","can"
"ID","258_christine_T04@2"

    Instances of ADVP function in the clause as adverbials. Section 4.3.4 lists node label extensions that indicate adverbial functions, all of which except -BNF can be used with ADVP. For example, the adverb phrase in (3.66) has directional function.

(3.66)
=N(" shall we go on up there ")
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","MD","shall"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","PRO","we"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","VB","go"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","ADVP-DIR","ADVP","ADVP","RP","on"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","ADVP-DIR","ADVP","RP","up"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","ADVP-DIR","ADV","there"
"ID","302_christine_T40@10"

    Adverbials can occur with selected functions -OB1 (primary object) and -PRD(2) (predicative).

(3.67)
=N(" it takes so long ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","it"
"IP-MAT","VBP","takes"
"IP-MAT","ADVP-OB1","ADVP","ADV","so"
"IP-MAT","ADVP-OB1","ADV","long"
"ID","99_christine_T04@7"
(3.68)
=N(" the game <apos>s over ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","the"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","N","game"
"IP-MAT","BEP","<apos>s"
"IP-MAT","ADVP-PRD2","RP","over"
"ID","404_christine_T24@9"

3.5   Preposition phrase (PP)

This section considers the form and function of preposition phrases.

3.5.1   PP heads

A preposition phrase (PP) has a preposition as its head and this is usually followed by a noun phrase (e.g. on the table). Some prepositions consist of more than one word and are called multi-word (or complex) prepositions. For example, adjacent_to, in_front_of.

3.5.2   PP dependents

The other phrase types of this chapter (NP, ADJP, and ADVP) can contain only the head word. A preposition phrase (PP) is different: There is normally a dependent. This happens because prepositions generally require a following noun phrase (NP) or clause (IP). When the dependent of a phrase is a necessary presence, the dependent is called a COMPLEMENT. For example, the PP out_of the box contains the preposition head word out_of and the NP complement the box, to give the structure of (3.69).

(3.69)
=N(" out of the box ")
"PP-DIR","P-ROLE","out_of"
"PP-DIR","NP","D","the"
"PP-DIR","NP","N","box"
"ID","7#215_samples_kisonihongo@1"

3.5.3   PP conjunction

Table 3.9: Tag for bare preposition phrase

PPpreposition phrase

Instances of PP are conjoined at the top level of their structure.

(3.70)
=N(" Across the Commonwealth and around the world ")

"PP-DIR","PP","NP","D","the"
"PP-DIR","PP","NP","NPR","Commonwealth"

"PP-DIR","CONJP","PP","P-ROLE","around"
"PP-DIR","CONJP","PP","NP","D","the"
"PP-DIR","CONJP","PP","NP","N","world"
"ID","2#12_texts_Queen_broadcast_5_April_2020@1,2,13"

"ADJP-PRD2","PP","CONJP","CONJ","but"
"ADJP-PRD2","PP","CONJP","ADVP-MOD","ADV","also"

"ID","33#70_susanne_J22@4,10,22"

"PP-DIR","CONJP","PP","NP","NS","parties"

3.5.4   PP functions

In the clause, a PP typically acts as an adverbial. All of the possible adverbial roles listed in section 4.3.4 can be used with PP. In addition, PP-OBU of section 4.3.2.3 is possible to make an unspecified object, which gains the content of its role from being a verb selected element.





Chapter 4
Clauses


4.1   Introduction

A clause is a matrix clause when it is not subordinate — that is, when it is not contained within a higher phrase or clause. Instances of clause subordination were seen in chapter 3 as modifiers and complements within phrases. Chapter 5 will present options for clause-within-clause subordination. In this chapter, section 4.2 gives an overview of annotation to distinguish matrix clause types. Section 4.3 covers options available for analysing clause function. Finally, section 4.4 introduces clause internal coordination with CONJP (conjunction phrase) and IML (clause intermediate) layers.


4.2   Matrix clause types

The internal form of a matrix clause can determine its use, and this gives rise to a basis for distinguishing the different clause types of:

  • declaratives (discussed in section 4.2.1),
  • interrogatives (discussed in section 4.2.2),
  • imperatives (discussed in section 4.2.3), and
  • sentence fragments (discussed in section 4.2.4).

4.2.1   Declaratives

Table 4.1: Tag for matrix clause

IP-MATmatrix clause

A basic matrix clause, given the tag of Table 4.1, is a declarative clause. A basic matrix clause requires a finite verb. For example, (4.1) has the past tense verb cried.

(4.1)
=N(" I cried my eyes out at that ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
"IP-MAT","VBD","cried"
"IP-MAT","NP-OB1","NP-GENV","PRO","my"
"IP-MAT","NP-OB1","NS","eyes"
"IP-MAT","ADVP-DIR","RP","out"
"IP-MAT","PP-CNT","P-ROLE","at"
"IP-MAT","PP-CNT","NP","D","that"
"ID","106_christine_T27@5"

Modal verbs only have finite forms. The short form 'll of WILL occurs as the finte verb of (4.2), with cry as a following infinitive lexical verb.

(4.2)
=N(" I <apos>ll cry some more if you # ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
"IP-MAT","MD","<apos>ll"
"IP-MAT","VB","cry"
"IP-MAT","NP-MNR","D","some"
"IP-MAT","NP-MNR","ADJP","ADJR","more"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-CNT-CND","P-CONN","if"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-CNT-CND","IP-ADV","NP-SBJ","PRO","you"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-CNT-CND","IP-ADV","PU","#"
"ID","431_christine_T21@5,7"

A finite DO can also occur with an infinitive lexical verb, as in (4.3), with past tense did acting to emphasise the kiss.

(4.3)
=N(" And he did kiss me . ")
"IP-MAT","CONJ","And"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{FORREST}","PRO","he"
"IP-MAT","DOD","did"
"IP-MAT","VB","kiss"
"IP-MAT","NP-OB1;{BERYL}","PRO","me"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","76_lob_N02@7,9"

    Basic declarative clauses have positive polarity. The polarity of a matrix clause is inverted by adding negation, either as the full form not (e.g., (4.4)) or the contracted n't (e.g., (4.5) and (4.6)). Both forms need a preceding (immediately preceding when there is contraction) modal verb or finite form of BE, HAVE, or DO.

(4.4)
=N(" This is not an acceptable turnover . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","This"
"IP-MAT","BEP;~Ln","is"
"IP-MAT","NEG","not"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","D","an"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","ADJP","ADJ","acceptable"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","N","turnover"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","112_bnc_B41@7,5"
(4.5)
=N(" I wo n<apos>t cry ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
"IP-MAT","MD","wo"
"IP-MAT","NEG","n<apos>t"
"IP-MAT","VB","cry"
"ID","466_christine_T21@7,5,9"
(4.6)
=N(" People do n<apos>t <bital> do <eital> that . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","N","People"
"IP-MAT","DOP","do"
"IP-MAT","NEG","n<apos>t"
"IP-MAT","PU","<bital>"
"IP-MAT","DO","do"
"IP-MAT","PU","<eital>"
"IP-MAT","NP-OB1","D","that"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","147_bnc_C02@7,5,11"

4.2.2   Interrogatives

Table 4.2: Tags for interrogative clause

CP-QUEquestion (direct or indirect)
IP-SUBclause complement of CP

Annotation for a matrix interrogative clause involves an IP-SUB clause layer that: (i) contains all of the clause content (except end punctuation), and (ii) projects a CP-QUE layer. A matrix interrogative is formed by having an initial clause component that is either:

    (i)
    a modal verb (e.g., (4.7)), or
    (ii)
    a finite form of BE, HAVE, or DO (e.g., (4.8) and (4.9)), or
    (iii)
    a constituent that includes a WH word (e.g. (4.10)–(4.15)).

A clause with (iii) is called a constituent question.

(4.7)
=N(" shall we go in Tesco <apos>s ")
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","MD","shall"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","PRO","we"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","VBP;~Ipr","go"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","PP-DIR","P-ROLE","in"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","PP-DIR","NP","NP-GENV","NPR","Tesco"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","PP-DIR","NP","NP-GENV","GENM","<apos>s"
"ID","173_christine_T36@3"
(4.8)
=N(" Is that still his position ? ")
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","BEP;~Ln","Is"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","P-ROLE","that"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","ADVP-TMP","ADV","still"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","NP-PRD2","NP-GENV","PRO","his"
"CP-QUE","IP-SUB","NP-PRD2","N","position"
"CP-QUE","PU","?"

For matrix constituent questions where the initial clause component is a non-subject, before the clause subject occurs, there needs to be an intervening modal verb or finite form of BE, HAVE, or DO, as seen with (4.11)–(4.15).

4.2.3   Imperatives

Table 4.3: Tag for imperative clause

IP-MAT-IMPimperative clause

An imperative clause requires a verb in the imperative form. The imperative form is a finite form of the verb that is typically equivalent to the infinitive form, and so the verb is tagged in the same way as an infinitive. It is appearance under an IP-MAT-IMP layer that distinguishes the verb as having imperative form.

    Another property that marks the imperative clause as a distinct matrix clause type is the typical absence of a subject constituent. Presence of a constituent with vocative function (as in (4.16) and (4.17)) is one way open for the construction to have a referenced ‘do-er’ or ‘be-er’ or ‘have-er’ for the verb.

(4.16)
=N(" Students <comma> always remember to include references . ")
"IP-MAT-IMP","NP-VOC","NS","Students"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PU","<comma>"
"IP-MAT-IMP","ADVP-MOD","ADV","always"
"IP-MAT-IMP","VB;~Tt","remember"
"IP-MAT-IMP","IP-INF-OB1","TO","to"
"IP-MAT-IMP","IP-INF-OB1","VB;~Tn","include"
"IP-MAT-IMP","IP-INF-OB1","NP-OB1","NS","references"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PU","."
"ID","82_samples_ENG1100@10,2"
(4.17)
=N(" Stop thief ")
"IP-MAT-IMP-OB1","VB;~I","Stop"
"IP-MAT-IMP-OB1","NP-VOC","N","thief"
"ID","95#13_classics_POTTER_1902@2,4"
(4.18)
=N(" <ldquo> You shut up ! ")
"IP-MAT-IMP","PUQ","<ldquo>"
"IP-MAT-IMP","NP-SBJ","PRO","You"
"IP-MAT-IMP","VB;~phr_Vp","shut"
"IP-MAT-IMP","RP","up"
"IP-MAT-IMP","PU","!"
"ID","11_susanne_N09@7,4"

4.2.4   Sentence fragments

Presence of either (i) a subject and a finite verb, or (ii) an imperative verb are sufficient materials to warrant marking the enclosing structure as a clause (IP). When there is not enough material in an utterance to create an IP, the utterance is marked as a sentence fragment with the tag of Table 4.4.

Table 4.4: Tag for sentence fragment
