Examples (4.19)–(4.23) are illustrative of possible FRAG utterances.

"FRAG","META","{pause}"
"FRAG","PP-DIR","P-ROLE","up"
"FRAG","PP-DIR","NP","D","the"
"FRAG","PP-DIR","NP","NS","stairs"
"ID","71_christine_T40@1"

Note how (4.22) involves clause internal coordination, with the inclusion of CONJP and IML projections, as explained below in section 4.4. Example (4.23) consists of a sentence fragment that forms a matrix question, and so there is projection of a CP-QUE layer above the FRAG layer.


4.3   Clause function

Besides clause level words (notably verbs, but also negation, adverb particles, and existential there), phrases may occur as components of annotated clause structure. To occur at the clause level, a phrase component requires a tag with an extension for a clause level function. Available clause level functions are:

  • subject (discussed in section 4.3.1),
  • object(s) (discussed in section 4.3.2),
  • predicative (discussed in section 4.3.3), and
  • adverbial(s) (discussed in section 4.3.4).

4.3.1   Subject

A subject is always required in a basic clause. The subject is typically the noun phrase corresponding to the ‘do-er’ or ‘be-er’ or ‘have-er’ of the verb. The subject can determine the form of the first verb instance of the clause (e.g. I am, he/she/it is, we/you (all)/they are). Also, with regards to form, pronoun subject forms I/we/he/she/they contrast with non-subject forms me/us/him/her/them.

    Subjects typically occur with a clause initial position. For a couple of untypical clause types, the subject can appear after an auxiliary. This holds especially for interrogatives, as in examples of section 4.2.2 above. Inversion of the subject and an auxiliary verb can also occur after a frontmost adverbial phrase with a (semi-)negative adverbial head such as hardly, scarcely, little, never, seldom, rarely), as in (4.24).

(4.24)
=N(" Never at any time does the user feel constrained . ")
"IP-MAT","ADVP-TMP","ADV","Never"
"IP-MAT","ADVP-TMP","PP","P-ROLE","at"
"IP-MAT","ADVP-TMP","PP","NP","D","any"
"IP-MAT","ADVP-TMP","PP","NP","N","time"
"IP-MAT","DOP","does"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","the"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","N","user"
"IP-MAT","VB;~La","feel"
"IP-MAT","ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","constrained"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","28_young_adult_E82@15,13"

Inversion of the subject with a modal verb or finite form of BE, HAVE, or DO also happens with and so reduced clauses:

(4.25)
=N(" By then Hez could see for himself <comma> and so could the others . ")
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","PP-TMP","P-ROLE","By"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","PP-TMP","ADVP","ADV","then"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","NP-SBJ","NPR","Hez"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","MD","could"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","VB","see"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","PP-BNF","P-ROLE","for"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","PP-BNF","NP","PNX","himself"
"IP-MAT","IML","PU","<comma>"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP","IML","ADVP-MNR","ADV","so"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP","IML","MD","could"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP","IML","NP-SBJ","D","the"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP","IML","NP-SBJ","NS","others"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","41_susanne_N13@34,32"

Also, the subject occurs after the verb in clauses with existential there.

(4.26)
=N(" There remain many political and administrative problems to be solved . ")
"IP-MAT","EX","There"

"ID","54_susanne_J22@4,6"

  • tag extension -ESBJ marks the subject of a clause with existential there, as in (4.26) above;
  • tag extension -CSBJ marks the subject it noun phrase of an it-cleft construction, as in (4.27); and

4.3.2   Object

An object can only occur when there is a transitive verb. The following properties are typical:

  • An object is normally a noun phrase that follows its transitive verb, and gives the content that the transitive verb is acting upon.
  • An object cannot be an adjective phrase (in contrast to predicatives).
  • An object can be turned into the subject of a corresponding passive clause (see section 6.8.3). Compare Everyone saw me with I was seen (by everyone).
  • An object pronoun is in the accusative case. For example, in He likes her and She likes him, the accusative forms her and him fill the object position.

There are two types of objects:

  • direct object (-OB1) (discussed in section 4.3.2.1), and
  • indirect object (-OB2) (discussed in section 4.3.2.2).

Also, section 4.3.2.3 notes the unspecified object function for preposition phrases.

4.3.2.1   Direct object

A direct object generally follows immediately after its transitive verb, except where an indirect object intervenes. In the annotation, -OB1 is the basic tag extension for marking a direct object. Other more specialised markings seen in table 4.6 relate to the direct object function in particular constructions:

  • a derived direct object marked -DOB1 occurs in a clause alongside a non-finite embedded clause marked -OB1, as with (4.30) and (4.31); and
  • tag extension -NOB1 marks a notional direct object that occurs in a clause with a provisional direct object it, marked -POB1, as with (4.32) and (6.52).
(4.30)
=N(" We want children to fulfil their potential . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","We"
"IP-MAT","VBP","want"
"IP-MAT","NP-DOB1","NS","children"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-OB1","TO","to"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-OB1","VB","fulfil"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-OB1","NP-OB1","NP-GENV","PRO","their"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-OB1","NP-OB1","N","potential"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","30_bnc_B41@7,10"
(4.31)
=N(" I could see the blood running down his chest . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"

"IP-MAT","NP-DOB1","D","the"
"IP-MAT","NP-DOB1","N","blood"
"IP-MAT","IP-PPL-OB1","VAG","running"
"IP-MAT","IP-PPL-OB1","PP-DIR","P-ROLE","down"
"IP-MAT","IP-PPL-OB1","PP-DIR","NP","NP-GENV","PRO","his"

4.3.2.2   Indirect object

An indirect object occurs after ditransitive verbs such as GIVE and TELL, and comes before the direct object. It conforms to the other criteria for objects, including the formation of passives.

Table 4.7: Extension tag for indirect object function

-OB2indirect object

While rare, it is possible for the indirect object to be the only object present in the clause, as in (4.34).

(4.34)
=N(" that <apos>s why I asked you ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","that"
"IP-MAT","BEP;~equ_w","<apos>s"
"IP-MAT","CP-QUE-PRD2","IP-SUB","ADVP-CNT","WADV","why"
"IP-MAT","CP-QUE-PRD2","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
"IP-MAT","CP-QUE-PRD2","IP-SUB","VBD;~Dn.*","asked"
"IP-MAT","CP-QUE-PRD2","IP-SUB","NP-OB2","PRO","you"
"ID","300_christine_T16@15,17"

4.3.2.3   Underspecified object

The unspecified object function of Table 4.8 occurs only with preposition phrases that have a verb selected function.

Table 4.8: Tag extension for unspecified object

-OBUunspecified object

When we look at verb codes in chapter 6, we will find that verbs selecting PP-OBU involve a code with pr, e.g., [Ipr], [Tn.pr], etc.

4.3.3   Predicative

A predicative can only occur as a clause element when selected by the verb. A predicative typically follows the verb and (if one is present) the direct object. All of the phrase types considered in chapter 3 are able to function as predicatives: noun phrase, adjective phrase, adverb phrase, and preposition phrase. This behaviour is in contrast to objects, which cannot be adjective phrases.

    There are two types of predicative: the subject predicative and the object predicative. Both types have the semantic role of characterising a preceding noun phrase:

  • A subject predicative adds more information about the subject, as seen with (4.35), while
  • an object predicative does the same for its direct object, as seen with (4.36).
(4.35)
=N(" The Skipjack became the fastest submarine ever built . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","The"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","N","Skipjack"
"IP-MAT","VBD;~Ln","became"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","D","the"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","ADJP","ADJS","fastest"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","N","submarine"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","IP-PPL","NP-LGS","*"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","IP-PPL","ADVP-TMP","ADV","ever"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","IP-PPL","VVN","built"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","46_susanne_A20@9"
(4.36)
=N(" I find this very interesting . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
"IP-MAT","VBP;~Cn.a","find"
"IP-MAT","NP-OB1","D","this"
"IP-MAT","ADJP-PRD","ADVP","ADV","very"
"IP-MAT","ADJP-PRD","ADJ","interesting"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","14_texts_TED_talk_11@10"

In the annotation, having 2 as part of the predicative tag, to give -PRD2, marks a subject predicative. Absence of 2, signals an object predicative.

Table 4.9: Extension tags related to predicative functions

-PRDobject predicative
-PRD2subject predicative

4.3.4   Adverbials

An adverbial is a phrase that is used to modify a verb or clause. It is typical for adverb phrases to be used as adverbials, but also many other types of phrases can be used in this way, including noun phrases, preposition phrases, and subordinate clauses.

    In (4.37), the preposition phrases in the face and with a pistol, the noun phrase last night, and the subordinate clause after robbing him of $18 at Franklin and Mount streets are all adverbials that modify struck.

(4.37)
=N(" A thug struck a cab driver in the face with a pistol last night after robbing him of <dollar> 18 at Franklin and Mount streets . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","A"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","N","thug"
"IP-MAT","VBD;~Tn.pr","struck"
"IP-MAT","NP-OB1","D","a"
"IP-MAT","NP-OB1","N","cab"
"IP-MAT","NP-OB1","N","driver"
"IP-MAT","PP-LOC","P-ROLE","in"
"IP-MAT","PP-LOC","NP","D","the"
"IP-MAT","PP-LOC","NP","N","face"
"IP-MAT","PP-MNR","P-ROLE","with"
"IP-MAT","PP-MNR","NP","D","a"
"IP-MAT","PP-MNR","NP","N","pistol"
"IP-MAT","NP-TMP","ADJP","ADJ","last"
"IP-MAT","NP-TMP","N","night"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","P-CONN","after"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL","VAG;~Tn.pr","robbing"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL","NP-OB1","PRO","him"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL","PP-OBU","P-ROLE","of"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL","PP-OBU","NP","N","<dollar>"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL","PP-OBU","NP","NUM","18"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL","PP-LOC","P-ROLE","at"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL","PP-LOC","NP","NML","NML","NP","NPR","Franklin"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL","PP-LOC","NP","NML","NML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL","PP-LOC","NP","NML","NML","CONJP","NP","N","Mount"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL","PP-LOC","NP","NS","streets"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","10_susanne_A19@7,16,24,32,38"

    The adverbials of (4.37) are all optional elements of clause structure. But also, on a verb specific basis, adverbials may have clause presence as required elements when expressing a particular semantic relation that the verb selects, as seen with the grammatical codes of [Ipr], [In/pr], [Tn.pr], and [Dn.pr] discussed in chapter 6.

    Adverbials need to be given functional information in the annotation. The full range of extensions for marking adverbial functions is given in Table 4.10.

Table 4.10: Tag extensions for marking adverbial functions

-ABSabsolute
-BNFbenefactive
-CNTcontingency
-COMcomitative
-DIRdirectional
-LOClocative
-MNRmanner/degree
-MODmodality
-RSTrespect
-TMPtemporal

    With the exception of -DIR, the adverbial functions of Table 4.10 typically mark phrases that are not selected by the verb. Even when not selected by the verb, adverbials still need to make a meaning contribution that is compatible with the meaning of the verb.

4.4   Clause internal coordination

Table 4.11: Tag for clause intermediate level

IMLclause intermediate level

Coordination joins together two or more constituents which are outside of each other and of ‘equal status’. For example, content for three clauses become one clause through coordination in (4.45).

(4.45)
=N(" Mr G is the headmaster <comma> Miss K is the deputy head and Mrs V is the school secretary . ")
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","NP-SBJ","N","Mr"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","NP-SBJ","NPR","G"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","BEP;~Ln","is"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","NP-PRD2","D","the"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","NP-PRD2","N","headmaster"
"IP-MAT","IML","PU","<comma>"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP;@3","IML","NP-SBJ","N","Miss"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP;@3","IML","NP-SBJ","NPR","K"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP;@3","IML","BEP;~Ln","is"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP;@3","IML","NP-PRD2","D","the"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP;@3","IML","NP-PRD2","N","deputy"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP;@3","IML","NP-PRD2","N","head"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP;@4","CONJ","and"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP;@4","IML","NP-SBJ","N","Mrs"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP;@4","IML","NP-SBJ","NPR","V"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP;@4","IML","BEP;~Ln","is"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP;@4","IML","NP-PRD2","D","the"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP;@4","IML","NP-PRD2","N","school"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP;@4","IML","NP-PRD2","N","secretary"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","3_age_11_H11@2,3,19,37"

The overall coordinated structure of (4.45) is gathered under a single IML (intermediate clause layer) projection with the external behaviour of a single clause. Each conjunct of the coordinated structure is an IML that is either:

    (i)
    the first conjunct followed by one or more CONJP sisters, or
    (ii)
    a conjunct that is directly under a CONJP.

    Coordinated structure can itself form the conjunct of larger coordinated structure. Such layered coordination is easiest to identify when different coordinators are used at each level, as (4.46) illustrates.

(4.46)
=N(" Mrs G was headmistress when I came but she left # soon after she left <comma> and a new headmistress came *<comma>* her name is Miss B . ")

"IP-MAT","IML","IML","IML","IML","NP-SBJ","NPR","G"

"IP-MAT","IML","IML","IML","IML","NP-PRD2","N","headmistress"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","IML","IML","PP-SCON-TMP","P-CONN","when"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","IML","IML","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-ADV","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","IML","IML","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-ADV","VBD;~I","came"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","IML","CONJP","CONJ","but"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","IML","CONJP","IML","FS","NP-SBJ","PRO","she"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","IML","CONJP","IML","FS","VBD","left"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","IML","CONJP","IML","FS","PU","#"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","IML","CONJP","IML","ADVP-TMP","ADVP","ADV","soon"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","IML","CONJP","IML","ADVP-TMP","ADV","after"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","IML","CONJP","IML","IP-ADV","NP-SBJ","PRO","she"
"IP-MAT","IML","IML","IML","CONJP","IML","IP-ADV","VBD;~I","left"
"IP-MAT","IML","PU","<comma>"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP","IML","IML","IML","NP-SBJ","D","a"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP","IML","IML","IML","NP-SBJ","ADJP","ADJ","new"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP","IML","IML","IML","NP-SBJ","N","headmistress"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP","IML","IML","IML","VBD;~I","came"
"IP-MAT","IML","CONJP","IML","IML","PU","_*<comma>*_"

"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","4_age_11_H04@3,4,51,52"

    Coordination can happen at different levels of clause structure. In (4.47) coordination occurs at the highest level of clause structure, so that the front placement of is (i.e. marking for an interrogative clause) is required for both conjuncts.

Chapter 5
Clause-within-clause subordination


5.1   Introduction

The hierarchy of layers from chapter 1 starts with a matrix clause as the highest layer with phrases subsequently reached. The picture of internal clause structure was fleshed out further in chapter 4 to include clause intermediate layers (IML) with coordination. The hierarchy from chapter 1 also allows two types of clause subordination:

  • clause-within-phrase, and
  • clause-within-clause.

Clause-within-phrase possibilities were seen in chapter 3 with discussions of modifiers and complements inside phrases. This chapter details clause-within-clause options. In chapter 4, clause elements were realised by (noun) phrases with subject, object, predicative, and adverbial roles. Now we will see how such elements can also be clauses themselves. The discussion will group subordinate clauses by the node labels of their maximal constituent:

  • IP-INF (section 5.2),
  • IP-PPL (section 5.3),
  • CP-THT (section 5.4),
  • CP-QUE (section 5.5), and
  • PP-SCON (section 5.6).

With the exception of CP-QUE, which is used as the tag for both direct and indirect questions, the other tags are used to label clauses that are incapable of standing alone as the main clause of a sentence. When they are all the content of an utterance, they are inside a FRAG layer.


5.2   IP-INF (infinitival clause)

Infinitival clauses are clauses that contain a verb in the infinitive form without support from a finite element like a dummy DO or modal verb (as seen with matrix clauses in section 4.2.1). Infinitival clauses have a maximal constituent with the tag of Table 5.1.

Table 5.1: Tag for infinitival clause

IP-INFinfinitival clause

There are two kinds of infinitival clause:

  • those that contain the infinitive marker to (tagged TO), typically as the clause initial word, and
  • those that lack the infinitive marker to, called bare infinitival clauses.

When occuring directly within a clause, the syntactic tag IP-INF needs a function extension. Possibilities are listed in Table 5.2.

Table 5.2: Tags for infinitival clauses with function extensions

IP-INF-CATcatenative
IP-INF-(N)SBJ(notional) subject
IP-INF-(N)OB1(notional) direct object
IP-INF-PRD2subject predicative
IP-INF-PRDobject predicative

Chapter 6 will show infinitival clauses as verb selected elements with direct object, object predicative, and catenative functions. Notably, instances of bare infinitival clauses occur as:

  • bare IP-INF-OB1 with code [Tni] (section 6.4.11),
  • bare IP-INF-PRD with code [Cn.i] (section 6.5.7), and
  • bare IP-INF-CAT with code [cat_i] (section sectionref:[__cat_ipattern__]).

Infinitival clauses with the infinitive marker to exhibit a fuller range of options, occurring as:

  • IP-INF-OB1 with code [It] (section 6.3.5).
  • IP-INF-OB1 with code [Tt] (section 6.4.6).
  • IP-INF-OB1 with code [Tnt] (section 6.4.7).
  • IP-INF-PRD with code [Cn.t] (section 6.5.5),
  • IP-INF-OB1 with code [Dn.t] (section 6.5.14),
  • IP-INF-OB1 with code [Dpr.t] (section 6.5.15), and
  • IP-INF-CAT with code [cat_t] (section sectionref:[__cat_tpattern__]).

The remainder of this section notes how infinitival clauses with the infinitive marker to can occur as subjects (section 5.2.1) and subject predicatives (section 5.2.2).

5.2.1   IP-INF with subject function

Infinitival clauses with the infinitive marker to can have subject function, as in (5.1).

(5.1)
=N(" To do so would make his job well<hyphen>nigh impossible . ")
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-SBJ","TO","To"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-SBJ","DO","do"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-SBJ","ADVP-MNR","ADV","so"
"IP-MAT","MD","would"
"IP-MAT","VB","make"
"IP-MAT","NP-OB1","NP-GENV","PRO","his"
"IP-MAT","NP-OB1","N","job"
"IP-MAT","ADJP-PRD","ADVP","ADV","well<hyphen>nigh"
"IP-MAT","ADJP-PRD","ADJ","impossible"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","135_susanne_N09@2"

It is typical for an infinitival clause with subject function to occur as an extraposed notional subject (-NSBJ). A notional subject occurs in combination with: (i) a provisional subject it, marked -PSBJ, as in (5.2); or (ii) a derived subject, marked -DSBJ, as in (5.3).

(5.2)
=N(" it <apos>s quite comfy to lay on it ")
"IP-MAT","NP-PSBJ","PRO","it"
"IP-MAT","BEP;~La","<apos>s"
"IP-MAT","ADJP-PRD2","ADVP","ADV","quite"
"IP-MAT","ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","comfy"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-NSBJ","TO","to"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-NSBJ","VB;~Ipr","lay"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-NSBJ","PP-LOC","P-ROLE","on"
"IP-MAT","IP-INF-NSBJ","PP-LOC","NP","PRO","it"
"ID","431_christine_T40@13,2"
(5.3)

5.3   IP-PPL (participial clause)

Participial clauses have a maximal constituent with the tag of Table 5.3.

Table 5.3: Tag for participial clause

IP-PPLparticipial clause

There are two kinds of participial clause:

  • those that contain a past participle ({en}) verb form, and
  • those that contain a present participial ({ing}) verb form.

When occuring directly within a clause, the syntactic tag IP-PPL needs a function extension. Possibilities are listed in Table 5.4.

Table 5.4: Tags for participial clauses with function extensions

IP-PPL-CATcatenative
IP-PPL-(N)SBJclause (notional) subject
IP-PPL-OB1direct object
IP-PPL-PRD2subject predicative
IP-PPL-PRDobject predicative

Chapter 6 will show participial clauses as verb selected elements with direct object, object predicative, and catenative functions. Notably, instances of participial clauses with a past participle ({en}) verb form occur as:

  • IP-PPL-PRD with codes [VP24A, VP24B, VP24C] (section 6.6), and
  • IP-PPL-CAT with code [cat_e] (section sectionref:[__cat_epattern__]).

Participial clauses with a present participial ({ing}) verb form exhibit a fuller range of options, occurring as:

  • IP-PPL-OB1 with code [Tg] (section 6.4.8),
  • IP-PPL-OB1 with code [Tsg] (section 6.4.9),
  • IP-PPL-OB1 with code [Tng] (section 6.4.10),
  • IP-PPL-PRD with code [Cn.g] (section 6.5.6), and
  • IP-PPL-CAT with code [cat_g] (section sectionref:[__cat_gpattern__]).

The remainder of this section notes how participial clauses can occur as subjects (section 5.3.1) and subject predicatives (section 5.3.2).

5.3.1   IP-PPL with subject function

Participial clauses with a present participial ({ing}) verb can have subject function, as in (5.7).

(5.7)
=N(" Shooting the reactor will damage it ")
"IML","IP-PPL-SBJ","VAG;~Tn","Shooting"
"IML","IP-PPL-SBJ","NP-OB1;{REACTOR}","D","the"
"IML","IP-PPL-SBJ","NP-OB1;{REACTOR}","N","reactor"
"IML","MD","will"
"IML","VB;~Tn","damage"
"IML","NP-OB1;{REACTOR}","PRO","it"
"ID","3#12_texts_THRUST@2"

A participial clause with a present participial ({ing}) verb can also occur as an extraposed notional subject (-NSBJ) in combination with a provisional subject it, as in (5.8).

(5.8)
=N(" <ldquo> It does n<apos>t seem quite right <comma> telling her a thing like that . ")
"IP-MAT","PUQ","<ldquo>"
"IP-MAT","NP-PSBJ","PRO","It"
"IP-MAT","DOP","does"
"IP-MAT","NEG","n<apos>t"
"IP-MAT","VB","seem"
"IP-MAT","ADJP-PRD2","ADVP","ADV","quite"
"IP-MAT","ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","right"
"IP-MAT","PU","<comma>"
"IP-MAT","IP-PPL-NSBJ","VAG","telling"
"IP-MAT","IP-PPL-NSBJ","NP-OB2","PRO","her"
"IP-MAT","IP-PPL-NSBJ","NP-OB1","D","a"
"IP-MAT","IP-PPL-NSBJ","NP-OB1","N","thing"
"IP-MAT","IP-PPL-NSBJ","NP-OB1","PP","P-ROLE","like"
"IP-MAT","IP-PPL-NSBJ","NP-OB1","PP","NP","D","that"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","17_susanne_N12@21,4"

5.3.2   IP-PPL with subject predicative function

When occuring with the copula verb BE, a participial clause with a present participial ({ing}) verb form can have subject predicative function, to thereby create an equative construction, as in (5.9). Equative constructions with a participial clause predicative with a present participial ({ing}) verb can be found by looking for the [equ_g] verb code.

(5.9)
=N(" The funniest job was cleaning a dog . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","The"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","ADJP","ADJS","funniest"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","N","job"
"IP-MAT","BED;~equ_g","was"
"IP-MAT","IP-PPL-PRD2","VAG;~Tn","cleaning"
"IP-MAT","IP-PPL-PRD2","NP-OB1","D","a"
"IP-MAT","IP-PPL-PRD2","NP-OB1","N","dog"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","2_age_09_M12@12"

5.4   CP-THT (that clause)

A that-clause is an embedded clause with a finite verb. This may have the complementizer that (tagged C) as the clause initial word. A that clause has a maximal constituent with the tag of Table 5.5 and, when structurally complete, contains a finite clause layer (IP-SUB) which in turn contains all the clause content, including any clause initial complementizer.

Table 5.5: Tag for that-clause

CP-THTTHAT clause

When occuring directly within a clause, the syntactic tag CP-THT needs a function extension. Possibilities are listed in Table 5.6.

Table 5.6: Tags for that-clauses with function extensions

CP-THT-(N)SBJ(notional) subject
CP-THT-(N)OB1(notional) direct object
CP-THT-PRD2subject predicative

Chapter 6 will show THAT clauses as verb selected elements with direct object function:

  • CP-THT-OB1 with code [Tf] (section 6.4.4),
  • CP-THT-OB1 with code [Dn.f] (section 6.5.10), and
  • CP-THT-OB1 with code [Dpr.f] (section 6.5.11).

The remainder of this section notes how that-clauses can occur as subjects (section 5.4.1), and subject predicatives (section 5.4.2).

5.4.1   CP-THT with subject function

That-clauses can have subject function, as in (5.10).

(5.10)
=N(" That she impressed me instantly was obvious ")
"IML","CP-THT-SBJ","IP-SUB","C","That"
"IML","CP-THT-SBJ","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","PRO","she"
"IML","CP-THT-SBJ","IP-SUB","VBD","impressed"
"IML","CP-THT-SBJ","IP-SUB","NP-OB1","PRO","me"
"IML","CP-THT-SBJ","IP-SUB","ADVP-TMP","ADV","instantly"
"IML","BED;~La","was"
"IML","ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","obvious"
"ID","3#27_susanne_N18@2"

It is typical for a that-clause with subject function to occur as an extraposed notional subject (-NSBJ) in combination with a provisional subject it, as in (5.11).

(5.11)
=N(" It is <comma> however <comma> quite obvious that these are bulk and impersonal e-mails ")
"IML","NP-PSBJ","PRO","It"
"IML","BEP;~La","is"
"IML","PU","<comma>"
"IML","ADVP-CNT","ADV","however"
"IML","PU","<comma>"
"IML","ADJP-PRD2","ADVP","ADV","quite"
"IML","ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","obvious"
"IML","CP-THT-NSBJ","IP-SUB","C","that"
"IML","CP-THT-NSBJ","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","D","these"
"IML","CP-THT-NSBJ","IP-SUB","BEP;~Ln","are"
"IML","CP-THT-NSBJ","IP-SUB","NP-PRD2","NML","NML","NP","N","bulk"
"IML","CP-THT-NSBJ","IP-SUB","NP-PRD2","NML","NML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
"IML","CP-THT-NSBJ","IP-SUB","NP-PRD2","NML","NML","CONJP","NP","ADJP","ADJ","impersonal"
"IML","CP-THT-NSBJ","IP-SUB","NP-PRD2","NS","e-mails"
"ID","3#32_young_adult_E84@20,2"

5.5   CP-QUE (indirect question)

Tags for indirect question with function extensions are given in Table 5.7.

Table 5.7: Tags for indirect questions with function extensions

Chapter 6 will show indirect questions as verb selected elements with direct object function:

  • CP-QUE-OB1 with code [Tw] (section 6.4.5),
  • CP-QUE-OB1 with code [Dn.w] (section 6.5.12), and
  • CP-QUE-OB1 with code [Dpr.w] (section 6.5.13).

The remainder of this section notes how indirect questions can occur as subjects (section 5.5.1) and subject predicatives (section 5.5.2).

5.6   PP-SCON (subordinate component of subordinate conjunction)

This section considers clauses that are the subordinate component of subordinate conjunctions. These clauses can differ with regards to:

  • the type of subordinate clause, that is, either IP-INF (sections 5.6.1 and 5.6.2), IP-PPL (sections 5.6.3 and 5.6.4), or IP-ADV (sections 5.6.5 and 5.6.6),
  • whether (sections 5.6.1, 5.6.3, and 5.6.5) or not (sections 5.6.2, 5.6.4, and 5.6.6) an explicit coordinating conjunction word is present (e.g., before, in_order, when; tagged P-CONN),
  • the adverbial function of the subordinate conjunction (see Table 5.9 below).

The annotation of the subordinate component always provides a maximal constituent layer with the tag of Table 5.8, which is headed by a coordinating conjunction word when present.

Table 5.8: Tag for subordinating conjunction clause

PP-SCONsubordinate component of subordinate conjunction

In addition, the syntactic tag PP-SCON needs a function extension. Possibilities are listed in Table 5.9.

Table 5.9: Tags for subordinating conjunction clauses with function extensions

PP-SCON-ABSabsolute adverbial
PP-SCON-CNT-CNDconditional
PP-SCON-CNTcontingency adverbial
PP-SCON-DIRdirectional adverbial
PP-SCON-LOClocative adverbial
PP-SCON-MNRmanner/degree adverbial
PP-SCON-MODmodality adverbial
PP-SCON-RSTrespect adverbial
PP-SCON-TMPtemporal adverbial

Note that the extension possibilities of Table 5.9 overlap with Table 4.10 of chapter 4.

5.6.1   IP-INF introduced by subordinating conjunction word

This section gives examples of infinitival clauses that occur with an explicit coordinating conjunction word to introduce them.

  • contingency adverbial:

Chapter 6
Verb codes


6.1   Introduction

It is the verb of a clause layer that determines which constituents (if any, besides the subject) are required to make the clause structurally complete. The annotation marks these requirements with a verb code added to the tag of each verb. The system of verb codes extends the verb code system of the fourth edition of the Oxford Advanced Learner's Dictionary (OALD4; Cowie 1989). These verb codes:

(i)
partially establish word sense,
(ii)
disambiguate the subject contribution for passivised verbs,
(iii)
signal when adverbial elements are selected items of the clause,
(iv)
provide a succinct notation to assist cataloging and searching clause structure, and
(v)
offer an independent check on the validity of the clause annotation.

6.1.1   OALD4 verb codes

An OALD4 verb code starts with a capital letter: L, I, T, C, or D. This capital letter signals how the verb belongs to one of the verb classes of Table 6.1. These verb classes set requirements for the structural level of the clause: the NUMBER of elements and their FUNCTION. An element with subject function is always required in a basic clause, so mentioned elements are additional to the subject.

Table 6.1: Capital letters L, I, T, C, and D

L LINKING verb is associated with a SUBJECT PREDICATIVE (-PRD2), an element which provides information about the subject of the clause.
I INTRANSITIVE verb is NOT associated with a PREDICATIVE or an OBJECT, although it may be associated with an ADVERBIAL, an element which tells us about time, place, manner, etc of the action of the verb.
T TRANSITIVE verb MONO-TRANSITIVE verb is associated with a DIRECT OBJECT (-OB1), an element which often refers to the person or thing affected by the action of the verb.
C COMPLEX-TRANSITIVE verb is associated with both a DIRECT OBJECT (-OB1) and an OBJECT PREDICATIVE (-PRD), an element which provides more information about the direct object. Note: in the code, a dot divides information about the realisation of the direct object from information about the realisation of the object predicative.
D DITRANSITIVE verb is associated with both a DIRECT OBJECT (-OB1) and an INDIRECT OBJECT (-OB2), an element which refers to a person who receives something or benefits from an action. Note: in the code, a dot divides information about the realisation of the direct object from information about the realisation of the indirect object.

    A verb code involves a capital letter from Table 6.1 possibly followed by lower case letters from Table 6.2, possibly separated by a dot (‘.’) character. These lower case letters represent information about the FORM of the phrases that are used to realise the required elements that are signalled by their capital letter. For example, [La] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a linking verb + subject predicative that is an adjective phrase, and [Cn.a] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a complex-transitive verb + direct object that is a noun phrase + object predicative that is an adjective phrase.

Table 6.2: Lower case letters a, n, p, pr, n/pr, n/a, t, f, w, g, and i

aadjective phrase
nnoun phrase
padverb particle
prpreposition phrase
n/prnoun phrase or preposition phrase
n/aas + noun phrase or adjective phrase
tnon-finite clause (to-infinitive) (IP-INF with internal TO)
fthat-clause (CP-THT)
wfinite or non-finite clause with wh (CP-QUE)
gnon-finite clause ({ing} form) (IP-PPL)
inon-finite clause (‘bare’ infinitive) (IP-INF without internal TO)

6.1.2   Orientation

The rest of this documentation is organised as follows. Section 6.2 presents linking verb codes.


6.2   Linking verb codes

6.2.1   [La]

[La] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a linking verb + subject predicative that is an adjective phrase. As Table 6.3 illustrates, the subject predicative describes some quality or feature of the subject (Cf Cn.a).

Table 6.3: [La]

subjectlinking verbsubject predicative (-PRD2): adjective phrase
1.The weatherwasquite good.
2.The detailsappearreasonable.
3.The beertastedawful.
4.Helookedso sad.
5.The situationbecamevolatile.
6.Stacey Blackgotcurious.
7.The bulletwentwide.
8.The formationremainedperfect.

    As an example of annotation, in (6.1), BE acts as a linking verb with the subject predicative quite good to add information about the subject, the weather.

(6.1)
=N(" The weather was quite good <comma> apart from the occasional shower . ")
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","The"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","N","weather"
"IP-MAT","BED;~La","was"
"IP-MAT","ADJP-PRD2","ADVP","ADV","quite"
"IP-MAT","ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","good"
"IP-MAT","PU","<comma>"
"IP-MAT","PP-MOD","P-ROLE","apart_from"
"IP-MAT","PP-MOD","NP","D","the"
"IP-MAT","PP-MOD","NP","ADJP","ADJ","occasional"
"IP-MAT","PP-MOD","NP","N","shower"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","10_age_12_F27@7,9"

    Extraposition of the subject is possible in a clause with the [La] pattern, as (7.19) shows.

(7.19)
=N(" it # it was n<apos>t funny that day before I went to the {unclear} that ear ")
"IP-MAT","NP-PSBJ","PRO","it_#_it"
"IP-MAT","BED;~La","was"
"IP-MAT","NEG","n<apos>t"
"IP-MAT","ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","funny"
"IP-MAT","NP-NSBJ","D","that"
"IP-MAT","NP-NSBJ","N","day"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","P-CONN","before"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-ADV","NP-SBJ;{Jean003}","PRO","I"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-ADV","VBD;~Ipr","went"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-ADV","PP-DIR","P-ROLE","to"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-ADV","PP-DIR","NP","D","the"
"IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-ADV","PP-DIR","NP","META","{unclear}_that_ear"
"ID","21_christine_T01@2,12"

In the annotation of (7.19), -NSBJ function information marks the extraposed subject, while -PSBJ marks the provisional subject it.

6.2.2   [Ln]

[Ln] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a linking verb + subject predicative that is a noun phrase. As Table 6.4 illustrates, the subject predicative gives further information about the subject (Cf Cn.n).

Table 6.4: [Ln]

subjectlinking verbsubject predicative (-PRD2): noun phrase
1.The hedgehogisa generalist species.
2.The messappearsthe very opposite of form.
3.Three quarters of a millionsoundsa lot.
4.Delosbecameone of the largest marketplaces in the empire.
5.John Richey's kickprovedthe winning field goal.
6.The dateremainedthe real problem.
7.The only reasonseemedthe crossing of two paths.

    As an example of annotation, in (6.3), REMAIN acts as a linking verb with the subject predicative the real problem to add information about the subject, the date.

(6.3)
=N(" But the date remained the real problem . ")
"IP-MAT","CONJ","But"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","the"
"IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","N","date"
"IP-MAT","VBD","remained"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","D","the"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","ADJP","ADJ","real"
"IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","N","problem"
"IP-MAT","PU","."
"ID","51_bnc_B13@9,11"

6.3   Intransitive verb codes

6.3.1   [I]

[I] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is an intransitive verb but no other post-verbal selected element(s).

Table 6.5: [I]

subjectintransitive verbadjunct: (adverbial phrase of location, manner, time, etc)
1.The numbers of personal dossiersrosedramatically.
2.Shenodded.
3.My hon. Friendhas been reading.
4.Culture and the artsflourishedonce again.
5.Another shopopened.
6.Smith and Jonesmet.
  • In this pattern, the verb is not followed by an object, a subject predicative, or a closely linked adjunct (Cf Ipr). Optional adverbial phrases of location, manner, time, etc can be used (e.g. dramatically).
  • Some verbs can be used in this pattern and the [Tn] pattern without a change of subject (or of verb meaning):
(6.4)
a.
My hon. Friend has been reading. [I]
b.
My hon. Friend has been reading my speech. [Tn]
  • Some verbs can be used in this pattern (with and linking conjuncts to form the subject) and in a corresponding [Ipr] pattern (with with following the verb).
    • (6.5)
    a.
    Smith and Jones met. [I]
    b.
    Smith met with Jones. [Ipr]

    6.3.2   [Ipr]

    [Ipr] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is an intransitive verb and post-verbal selected preposition phrase.

    Table 6.6: [Ipr]

    subjectintransitive verbadjunct: preposition phrase
    1.The current monthly payrollcomesto about $15,000,000.
    2.These reportsreferto a level of secondary prevention.
    3.Any person making a requestshall complywith the procedures.
    4.Peoplecomplainabout immigrants coming here and taking away “our” jobs.
    5.Mr Macmillanwill relyon a hand-picked team.
    6.Her nosewas runningwith snot.
    • Here, the verb is closely linked in grammar and meaning to a preposition phrase. The exact choice of preposition is shown in bold print in the above table (Cf Tn.pr).
    • After some verbs, the preposition phrase cannot be removed without changing the meaning of the verb or producing nonsense (*):
    (6.6)
    a.
    The minister referred to the importance of exports.
    b.
    *The minister referred.
    • After other verbs, the preposition phrase can be removed freely:
    (6.7)
    a.
    Mother can't cope with the extra visitors.
    b.
    Mother can't cope.
    • After some verbs, a to-infinitive or {ing} form can be added to the preposition phrase:
    (6.8)
    a.
    You can't rely on Martin.
    b.
    You can't rely on Martin to help.
    • Some verbs used in this pattern can be made passive. The noun phrase following the preposition in the active pattern becomes the subject of the passive one:
    (6.9)
    a.
    The minister referred to the importance of exports.
    b.
    The importance of exports was referred to (by the minister).

    6.3.3   [Ip]

    [Ip] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is an intransitive verb and post-verbal selected adverb particle.

    Table 6.7: [Ip]

    subjectintransitive verbadjunct: adverb particle
    1.The soundsdiedout.
    2.The girlsjoinin.
    3.Another warning lightflashedon.
    4.Hephonesup.
    5.Sheturnedaside.
    6.Automatic controlstookover.
    • Here the verb is closely linked to an adverb particle. The exact choice of particle is shown in bold print in the above table (Cf Tn.p).
    • After some verbs, the particle cannot be removed without changing the meaning of the verb or producing nonsense (*):
    (6.10)
    a.
    A tiger has got out.
    b.
    *A tiger has got.
    • After some verbs, the particle can be deleted freely:
    (6.11)
    a.
    The noise faded away. [Ip]
    b.
    The noise faded. [I]

    In specific uses, a number of verbs include a preposition as a particle, for example, turn on, brighten up. These are often called phrasal verbs (or sometimes preposition verbs).

        The particle behaves as part of the verb, and when the verb has an object, this can occur before the particle, for example, turn the light on, brighten the room up, in contrast with PP dependents, for example, depend on the team, but not *depend the team on.

        Also, particles must precede an unstressed pronoun object, for example, turn it on but not *turn on it.

    6.3.4   [In/pr]

    [In/pr] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is an intransitive verb and post-verbal selected element that is either a noun phrase or a preposition phrase.

    Table 6.8: [In/pr]

    subjectintransitive verbadjunct: noun phrase/preposition phrase
    1.The downcounterreadszero.
    2.Heretreateda step.
    3.This operationlasted(for) 29 minutes.
    4.It's volume of beer productionrose(by) 3.5 per cent.
    • Here, the verb is closely linked to a noun phrase or preposition phrase which indicates ‘extent’ (e.g. how much the subject costs, what it measures, how long it lasts).
    • The correct choice of preposition is for or by.

    6.3.5   [It]

    [It] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is an intransitive verb + closely linked to-infinitive clause.

    Table 6.9: [It]

    subjectintransitive verbadjunct: non-finite clause (to-infinitive)
    1.Wewill not hesitateto go further.
    2.Ilongedto be nearer to it.
    3.The birdsdid not careto sing in it.
    4.The fundamental differencesremainto be elucidated.
    • Here, an intransitive verb is closely linked to a to-infinitive clause.
    • Verbs in this pattern cannot be made passive.
    (6.12)
    =N(" She goes to bed at about 8.00 P<slash>M ")
    "IML","NP-SBJ","PRO","She"
    "IML","VBP;~Ipr","goes"
    "IML","PP-DIR","P-ROLE","to"
    "IML","PP-DIR","NP","N","bed"
    "IML","PP-TMP","P-ROLE","at"
    "IML","PP-TMP","NP","ADVP","ADV","about"
    "IML","PP-TMP","NP","NUM","8.00"
    "IML","PP-TMP","NP","N","P<slash>M"
    "ID","3#9_age_12_F26@5,7"

    6.4   Mono-transitive verb codes

    6.4.1   [Tn]

    [Tn] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a monotransitive verb + direct object that is a noun phrase.

    Table 6.10: [Tn]

    subjecttransitive verbdirect object: noun phrase
    1.Shewatchedhis deft slender hands bleached by the light.
    2.My hon. Friendhas been readingmy speech.
    3.The poorpaidexactly the same as the rich.
    4.Ihaven't gotanything.
    5.Gabrielleopenedanother shop.
    • The direct object is a noun phrase (Cf Dn.n)
    • For verbs used in this pattern and the [I] pattern without a change of subject or meaning see section 6.3.1.
    • Some verbs used in this pattern can also be used on the [I] pattern only with what is the object of the [Tn] needing to become the subject of the [I] pattern.
    (6.13)
    a.
    Gabrielle opened another shop.
    b.
    Another shop opened.
    • Most verbs in this pattern can be made passive, with the object of the active pattern becoming the subject of the passive one:
    (6.14)
    a.
    A small boy opened the door.
    b.
    The door was opened (by a small boy).

    There are exception where no passive is possible [Tn no passive].

    6.4.2   [Tn.pr]

    [Tn.pr] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a monotransitive verb + direct object that is a noun phrase + an adverbial preposition phrase that is a selected item of the verb.

    Table 6.11: [Tn.pr]

    subjecttransitive verbdirect object: noun phraseadjunct: preposition phrase
    1.The teacherreferredthe classto a passage in the textbook.
    2.The waiterservedSarahwith a double helping.
    3.The councilhave clearedthe pavementsof rubbish.
    4.The lecturerconfusedyour name with mine.
    5.The visiting speakerthankedthe chairmanfor his kind remarks.
    • In this pattern, the verb is closely linked in grammar and meaning to a preposition phrase. The exact choice of preposition is shown in bold print in the above table (Cf Ipr).
    • After some verbs, the preposition phrase cannot be removed without changing the meaning of the verb or producing nonsense (*):
    (6.15)
    a.
    The teacher referred the class to a passage in the textbook.
    b.
    *The teacher referred the class.
    • After other verbs, the preposition phrase can be removed without changing the meaning of the verb:
    (6.16)
    a.
    The visiting speaker thanked the Chairman for his kind remarks. [Tn.pr]
    b.
    The visiting speaker thanked the Chairman. [Tn]
    • Most verbs in this pattern can be made passive. The direct object of an active verb becomes the subject of the same verb in the passive:
    (6.17)
    a.
    The council have cleared the pavements of rubbish.
    b.
    The pavements have been cleared of rubbish (by the council).

    There are exceptions where no passive is possible [Tn.pr no passive].

    6.4.3   [Tn.p]

    [Tn.p] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a monotransitive verb + direct object that is a noun phrase + selected adverb particle.

    Table 6.12: [Ln.p]

    subjecttransitive verbdirect object: noun phraseadjunct: adverb particle
    1.Heturnedthe radioon.
    2.An automatic safety systemshutthe plantdown.
    3.Helinedusup.
    4.One barrel explosioncan seta chain reactionoff.
    • In this pattern, the verb is closely linked to an adverb particle. The exact choice of particle is shown in bold print in the above table (Cf Ip).
    • After some verbs, the particle cannot be removed without changing the meaning of the verb or producing nonsense (*):
    (6.18)
    a.
    He turned the radio on.
    b.
    He turned the radio.
    • After other verbs, the particle can be deleted freely:
    (6.19)
    a.
    An automatic safety system shut the plant down.
    b.
    An automatic safety system shut the plant.
    • When the direct object is a pronoun, it precedes the particle:
    (6.20)
    He lined us up.

    When it is a short noun phrase, it can usually either precede or follow the particle:

    (6.21)
    a.
    One barrel explosion can set a chain reaction off.
    b.
    One barrel explosion can set off a chain reaction.

    When the direct object is a long noun phrase, it usually follows the particle:

    (6.22)
    =N(" But as they went out of the room <comma> he turned on the radio that was sitting on his desk . ")
    "IP-MAT","CONJ","But"
    "IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","P-CONN","as"
    "IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-ADV","NP-SBJ;{MOTHER+DOCTOR}","PRO","they"
    "IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-ADV","VBD","went"
    "IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-ADV","PP-DIR","P-ROLE","out_of"
    "IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-ADV","PP-DIR","NP","D","the"
    "IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-ADV","PP-DIR","NP","N","room"
    "IP-MAT","PU","<comma>"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DOCTOR}","PRO","he"
    "IP-MAT","VBD","turned"
    "IP-MAT","RP","on"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","D","the"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","N","radio"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","IP-REL","NP-SBJ","RPRO","that"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","IP-REL","BED;~cat_g","was"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","IP-REL","IP-PPL-CAT","VAG","sitting"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","IP-REL","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-LOC","P-ROLE","on"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","IP-REL","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-LOC","NP","NP-GENV;{DOCTOR}","PRO","his"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","IP-REL","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-LOC","NP","N","desk"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "ID","232_texts_TED_talk_11@30"
    • Most verbs in this pattern can be made passive. The object of an active verb becomes the subject of the same verb in the passive:
    (6.23)
    a.
    One barrel explosion can set a chain reaction off.
    b.
    A chain reaction can be set off (by one barrel explosion).

    There are exceptions where no passive is possible [Tn.p no passive].

    6.4.4   [Tf]

    [Tf] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a monotransitive verb + direct object that is a that-clause.

    Table 6.13: [Tf]

    subjecttransitive verbdirect object: that-clause
    1.Governor Tawesannouncedthat a triple swearing-in ceremony will be held in his office next Friday.
    2.Idon't thinkI am big.
    3.Heacknowledgesthat property relations make society unequal to begin with.
    4.Chromatography of whole serarevealedthat the areas of Rh antibody activity were generally continuous and wide.
    5.The lawrequiresyou remain stopped as long as the red lights are flashing.
    6.The high quality of the seam on Milosensuredthat the area remained popular with early travelers .
    • In this pattern, the direct object is a that-clause (Cf Dn.f, Dpr.f).
    • The complementizer that can sometimes be omitted.
    • Some verbs used in this pattern can be made passive. (Note the construction with it.)
    (6.24)
    a.
    The law requires that you remain stopped.
    b.
    That you remain stopped is required (by law).
    c.
    It is required (by law) that you remain stopped.
    (6.25)
    =N(" The law requires you remain stopped as long as the red lights are flashing -LRB- CVC 22454 -RRB- . ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","The"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","N","law"
    "IP-MAT","VBP;~Tf","requires"

    6.4.5   [Tw]

    [Tw] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a monotransitive verb + direct object that is a finite or non-finite clause with wh.

    Table 6.14: [Tw]

    subjecttransitive verbdirect object: finite or non-finite clause with wh
    1.Idon't knowwhat I should do about this one/what to do about this one.
    2.MI5 alonedetermineswho and which organisations are judged to be subversive/who and which organisations to judge to be subversive.
    3.This exampleillustrateshow the Report Program Generator simplifies the preparation/how to simplify the preparation.
    4.A small groupdecidewhat products they will sell/what products to sell.
    • In this pattern, the direct object is a finite or non-finite clause beginning with

    EITHER (i) A wh-element, which can be a pronoun (who(m), whose, which, what), or a determiner + noun (which roads, what time), or an adverb (why (finite clauses only), when, where, how);

    OR (ii) One of the conjunctions if (finite clauses only) or whether (Cf Dn.w, Dpr.w).

    • Some verbs used in this pattern can be made passive. (Note the construction with it.)
    (6.34)
    a.
    The group hasn't decided what products they will sell/what products to sell.
    b.

    c.
    It hasn't been decided (by the group) what products they will sell/what products to sell.

    There are exceptions [Tw no passive].

    6.4.6   [Tt]

    [Tt] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a monotransitive verb + direct object that is a non-finite clause (to-infinitive).

    Table 6.15: [Tt]

    subjecttransitive verbdirect object: non-finite clause (to-infinitive)
    1.Helovesto play with you.
    2.American newspaper reviewersliketo call his plays nihilistic.
    3.Ihateto have these things come up again and again.
    4.Three consecutive pinch-hittersfailedto produce.
    5.Ididn't wantto lose any more teeth.
    6.These societiescan expectto face difficult times.
    7.Imust rememberto take that film out.
    8.Wedon't needto pay.
    • In this pattern, the direct object is a no-finite clause consisting of or containing a to-infinitive (Cf Dn.t, Dpr.t).
    • After remember and forget, the contrast between the to-infinitive and the {ing} form corresponds to a difference in meaning:
    (6.35)
    a.
    I remembered to post your letters. [Tt]
    (= ‘I didn't forget to post them’)
    b.
    I remembered posting your letters. [Tg]
    (= ‘I recalled having posted them’)
    • For need, require, want see [Tg], note (c).
    • Verbs in this pattern cannot be made passive.
    (6.36)
    =N(" They were trying their best to be honest . ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","They"

    6.4.7   [Tnt]

    Table 6.16: [Tnt]

    subjecttransitive verbdirect object: non-finite clause (noun phrase + to-infinitive)
    1.Helovespeople to make a fuss of him.
    2.Shelikedhim to play the piano.
    3.Theypreferthe sufferer to be in institutional care.
    4.Wewantyou to know why your support of Goodwill is so important.
    5.Onewould not expectsuch an event to occur in animals possessing lungs of types I or II.
    • In this pattern, the direct object is a non-finite clause consisting of a to-infinitive introduced by a noun phrase. The noun phrase is the subject of the whole non-finite clause.
    • Some verbs in this pattern are made passive. The subject of the non-finite clause becomes the subject of the whole passive sentence:
    (6.37)
    a.
    I expect the parcel to arrive tomorrow.
    b.
    The parcel is expected to arrive tomorrow.

    There are exceptions [Tnt no passive].

    6.4.8   [Tg]

    Table 6.17: [Tg]

    subjecttransitive verbdirect object: non-finite clause {ing} form)
    1..
    2..
    3..
    4..
    5..
    6..
    7..
    8..
    • In this pattern, the direct object is a non-finite clause consisting of, or containing, an {ing} form.
    • For remember and forget, see [Tt], note (b).
    • After need, require and want (as in This shirt wants washing), the {ing} form of the verb can be replaced by the passive to-infinitive:
    (6.38)
    a.
    The children will still need looking after. [Tg]
    b.
    The children will still need to be looked after. [Tf]
    • This construction has no corresponding passive construction.

    6.4.9   [Tsg]

    Table 6.18: [Tsg]

    subjecttransitive verbdirect object: non-finite clause (noun phrase/possesive + {ing} form)
    1..
    2..
    3..
    4..
    5..
    • In this pattern, the direct object is a non-finite clause consisting of the {ing} form of a verb introduced EITHER by a noun phrase (e.g., him, her, us, John, the staff OR by a possesive form (e.g., his, her, our, John's, the staff's). The introductory noun phrase is the subject of the whole non-finite clause.
    • The possesive form at the beginning of the direct object is more formal than a noun phrase.
    • The verbs in this pattern cannot normally be made passive.

    6.4.10   [Tng]

    Table 6.19: [Tng]

    subjecttransitive verbdirect object: non-finite clause (noun phrase + {ing} form)
    1..
    2..
    3..
    4..
    5..
    • In this pattern, the direct object is a non-finite clause, in which a noun phrase introduces the {ing} form of the verb.
    • Most verbs in this pattern are ‘perception’ verbs. Of these, see, hear, feel, watch, notice, overhear and observe are also used in the [Tni] pattern:
    (6.39)
    a.
    The rescuers felt John losing his grip of the rope. [Tng]
    b.
    The rescuers felt John lose his grip of the rope. [Tni]

    Using the ‘bare’ infinitive here [Tni] implies that John fully lost his hold of the rope while the rescuers were in contact with him. The {ing} form [Tng] does not imply this.

    • Verbs in this pattern can be made passive. The noun phrase introducing the {ing} form becomes the subject of the whole passive sentence:
    (6.40)
    a.
    The children saw the cat stealing the meat.
    b.
    The cat was seen stealing the meat (by the children).

    Exceptions are shown in dictionary entries thus: [Tng no passive]

    6.4.11   [Tni]

    Table 6.20: [Tni]

    subjecttransitive verbdirect object: non-finite clause (noun phrase + ‘bare’ infinitive)
    1..
    2..
    3..
    4..
    5..
    • In this pattern, the direct object is a non-finite clause, in which a noun phrase introduces a ‘bare’ infinitive (the infinitive without to).
    • All the verbs used in this pattern are verbs of perception. They are watch, hear, see, feel, notice, overhear and observe. All are used in the [Tng] pattern also (see [Tng], note (b)).
    • Except for watch and notice, verbs in this pattern can be made passive. The noun phrase introducing the bare infinitive becomes the subject of the whole passive sentence, while the bare infinitive itself (e.g. slam, steal) becomes the to-infinitive (e.g. to slam, to steal):
    (6.43)
    a.
    The porter heard someone slam the door.
    b.
    Someone was heard to slam the door (by the porter).

    Exceptions are shown in dictionary entries thus: [Tni no passive]


    6.5   Complex-transitive verb codes

    6.5.1   [Cn.a]

    [Cn.a] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a complex-transitive verb + direct object that is a noun phrase + object predicative that is an adjective phrase.

    Table 6.21: [Cn.a]

    subjectcomplex-transitive verbdirect object: noun phraseobject predicative: adjective phrase
    1.Shethoughtsuch an expedition as thisutterly ridiculous.
    2.Some peoplemay findrude jokesfunny.
    3.88 &percent;havenursery educationavailable to them.
    4.A metal frameheldher headrigid.
    5.A good formulationwill keepthe clothesclean and white.
    6.The CEGBmadethe situationslightly worse.
    7.The governmentmight declarethe whole rallyillegal.
    • In this pattern, the object predicative is an adjective phrase which describes a feature or quality of the direct object (Cf La).
    • Many verbs in this pattern can be made passive. The direct object of an active verb becomes the subject of the same verb in the passive:
    (6.44)
    a.
    The CEGB made the situation slightly worse.
    b.
    The situation was made slightly worse (by the CEGB).

    There are exceptions where no passive is possible [Cn.a no passive].

    6.5.2   [Cn.n]

    [Cn.n] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a complex-transitive verb + direct object that is a noun phrase + object predicative that is a noun phrase.

    Table 6.22: [Cn.n]

    subjectcomplex-transitive verbdirect object: noun phraseobject predicative: noun phrase
    1.The community structuremakesthisthe best form of organisation.
    2.Taronamedthe dogPon.
    3.A test resultratedPacbasethe number one software engineering system.
    4.Hecallshis productionsdance-theatre works of motion, shape, light, and sound.
    5.Theyfoundhera great help.
    • In this pattern, the object predicative is a noun phrase which indicates the role, name, status, etc of the direct object (Cf Ln).
    • Many verbs in this pattern can be made passive. The direct object of an active verb becomes the subject of the same verb in the passive:
    (6.45)
    a.
    The court considered Smith a trustworthy witness.
    b.
    Smith was considered a trustworthy witness (by the court).

    There are exceptions where no passive is possible [Cn.n no passive].

        A main verb that takes an object predicative is called a complex transitive verb. It is typical for an object predicative to immediately follow the direct object. For example, in (6.46) FIND acts as a complex transitive with object predicative a great help to add more information about the direct object, her. Other verbs able to act as complex transitives — and so require an object predicative — are MAKE, CONSIDER, and NAME.

    (6.46)
    =N(" They found her a great help . ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","They"
    "IP-MAT","VBD;~Cn.n","found"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","PRO","her"
    "IP-MAT","NP-PRD","D","a"
    "IP-MAT","NP-PRD","ADJP","ADJ","great"
    "IP-MAT","NP-PRD","N","help"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "ID","10_samples_ENG1100@5,7,10"

    In contast to (6.46), a great belt in (6.47) is an object. This contrast illustrates how a predicative provides additional information about another entity in the clause, while an object will refer to a different entity (when not a reflexive or reciprocal pronoun).

    (6.47)
    =N(" They found her a great belt . ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","They"
    "IP-MAT","VBD;~Dn.n","found"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB2","PRO","her"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","D","a"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","ADJP","ADJ","great"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","N","belt"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "ID","11_samples_ENG1100@5,7,10"

    This contrast also shows how verbs will have different meanings when selecting either objects or predicatives: In (6.46), FIND has the meaning ‘become aware of’; In (6.47), FIND has the meaning ‘discover something after search’.

    6.5.3   [Cn.n/a]

    [Cn.n/a] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a complex-transitive verb + direct object that is a noun phrase + object predicative that is as + noun phrase/adjective phrase.

    Table 6.23: [Cn.n/a]

    subjectcomplex-transitive verbdirect object: noun phraseobject predicative: as + noun phrase/adjective phrase
    1.The Prime Minister's reactionwas to describethe September sittingas a “rigmarole”.
    2.The select committee reportidentifiedLiverpool Bayas a prime example of a sour gas project.
    3.Most scientistswould acceptmaterial benefitsas payment for their work.
    4.Governor TawesappointedLloyd L. Simpkinsas Maryland's Secretary of State.
    • In this pattern, the object predicative tells us how the direct object is regarded, judged, etc, or what he, she, or it is chosen to act or serve as.
    • The first word in the object predicative is always as.
    • A passive construction is possible for all verbs in this pattern except have. The direct object of an active verb becomes the subject of the same verb in the passive:
    (6.48)
    a.
    The police didn't accept the story as genuine.
    b.
    The story wasn't accepted as genuine (by the police).

    6.5.4   [Cn.pr]

    [Cn.pr] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a complex-transitive verb + direct object that is a noun phrase + object predicative that is a preposition phrase.

    Table 6.24: [Cn.pr]

    subjectcomplex-transitive verbdirect object: noun phraseobject predicative: preposition phrase
    1.I've leftthe girlson their own.
    2.Theyinvolvedpeoplein the process.
    3.One beaknikinvolvedthe womanin drugs and self-analysis.

    6.5.5   [Cn.t]

    Table 6.25: [Cn.t]

    subjectcomplex-transitive verbdirect object: noun phraseobject complement: non-finite clause (to-infinitive)
    1.COBOLpermitsa programmerto use language based on English words and phrases.
    2.Sheinspiredhimto play the piano.
    3..
    4..
    • In this pattern, the object complement is a to-infinitive, either alone or as part of a larger clause. It tells us what the object is made or helped to do or be.
    • The verbs in this pattern can be made passive. The direct object of an active verb becomes the subject of the same verb in the passive:
    (6.49)
    a.
    An official declared the place to be free of infection.
    b.
    The place was declared to be free of infection (by an official).

    Exceptions are shown in dictionary entries thus: [Cn.t no passive]

    6.5.6   [Cn.g]

    Table 6.26: [Cn.g]

    subjectcomplex-transitive verbdirect object: noun phraseobject complement: non-finite clause ({ing} form)
    1..
    2..
    3..
    4..
    5..
    6..
    • In this pattern, the object complement is the {ing} form of a verb, either alone (e.g. thinking) or as part of a longer clause (e.g. playing in the garden). It tells us what the object is made to do or is kept doing.
    • Only the verbs shown in the table are used in this pattern. Of these, set, have. get and start are ‘causative’ verbs (ie verbs meaning ‘cause something to happen’).
    • Except for have and start, the verbs can be made passive. The direct object of an active verb becomes the subject of the same verb in the passive:
    (6.50)
    a.
    The policeman got the traffic moving.
    b.
    The traffic was got moving (by the policeman).

    6.5.7   [Cn.i]

    Table 6.27: [Cn.i]

    subjectcomplex-transitive verbdirect object: noun phraseobject complement: non-finite clause (‘bare’ infinitive)
    1.The metallic smell of the gold lacemadeherwant to sneeze.
    2..
    3..
    4..
    • In this pattern, the object complement is the ‘bare’ infinitive (the infinitive without to), either alone (e.g. work) or as part of a larger clause (e.g. play in the road). It tells us what the object is made or allowed to do.
    • Only the verbs shown in the table are used in this pattern.
    • The verbs make and help can be made passive, but when they are, they are followed by a to-infinitive as in pattern [Cn.t]:
    (6.51)
    a.
    He was made to work (by his tutor).
    b.
    We were helped to organise the party (by Stephen).

    The other verbs shown here cannot be made passive.

    Introductory it may also be used to anticipate a following object clause where there is an intervening obligatory clause element. In this case there is no alternative to extraposition and the verb is marked with [Cn.a] code:

    (6.52)
    =N(" I found it strange that neither I nor the day seemed in a mourning mood ")
    "IML","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
    "IML","VBD;~Cn.a","found"
    "IML","NP-POB1","PRO","it"
    "IML","ADJP-PRD","ADJ","strange"
    "IML","CP-THT-NOB1","IP-SUB","C","that"
    "IML","CP-THT-NOB1","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","NML","CONJ","neither"
    "IML","CP-THT-NOB1","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","NML","NP","PRO","I"
    "IML","CP-THT-NOB1","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","CONJ","nor"
    "IML","CP-THT-NOB1","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","NP","D","the"
    "IML","CP-THT-NOB1","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","NML","CONJP","NP","N","day"
    "IML","CP-THT-NOB1","IP-SUB","VBD","seemed"
    "IML","CP-THT-NOB1","IP-SUB","PP-MNR","P-ROLE","in"
    "IML","CP-THT-NOB1","IP-SUB","PP-MNR","NP","D","a"
    "IML","CP-THT-NOB1","IP-SUB","PP-MNR","NP","ADJP","ADJ","mourning"
    "IML","CP-THT-NOB1","IP-SUB","PP-MNR","NP","N","mood"
    "ID","3#79_classics_JOYCE_1914@7,13"

    In (6.52) the NOB1 clause is a CP-THT, and the POB1 is it. The NOB1 clause can also be an IP-INF in which case the derived object is NP-DOB1 which then acts as a controller into the IP-INF-NOB1, as (6.53) demonstrates.

    (6.53)
    =N(" found the orders process hard to take ")
    "IML","VBD","found"
    "IML","NP-DOB1","D","the"
    "IML","NP-DOB1","NS","orders"
    "IML","NP-DOB1","N","process"
    "IML","ADJP-PRD","ADJ","hard"
    "IML","IP-INF-NOB1","NP-LGS","*"
    "IML","IP-INF-NOB1","TO","to"
    "IML","IP-INF-NOB1","VB","take"
    "ID","14#10_bnc_C03@14,4"

    6.5   Ditransitive verb codes

    6.5.8   [Dn.n]

    [Dn.n] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a ditransitive verb + indirect object that is a noun phrase + direct object that is a noun phrase.

    Table 6.28: [Dn.n]

    subjectditransitive verbindirect object (-OB2): noun phrasedirect object (-OB1): noun phrase
    1.The Prime Ministerhas offeredcommonwealth governmentsevery facility possible.
    2.Some major organisationsgivestaffadditional compensation.
    3.The old chaptaughthima great deal.
    4.The local centre for the handicappedlentthema wheelchair.
    5.Theyfoundhera great belt.
    6.I'll makeyoua nice cup of tea.
    7.Daddyboughtmea new bike.
    • This pattern has an indirect object (without a preposition) followed by a direct object. Both are noun phrases. The indirect but not the direct object can also be a personal pronoun (Cf Tn).
    • When the indirect object refers to someone who receives something, this pattern can usually be changed to the [Dn.pr] pattern with to:
    (6.54)
    a.
    Some major organisations give staff additional compensation. [Dn.n]
    b.
    Some major organisations give additional compensation to staff. [Dn.pr]
    • When the indirect object refers to someone who is expected to benefit from the action of the verb, the [Dn.n] pattern can be changed to the [Dn.pr] with for:
    (6.55)
    a.
    I'll make you a nice cup of tea. [Dn.n]
    b.
    I'll make a nice cup of tea for you. [Dn.pr]
    • For reasons why the [Dn.pr] pattern (with to or for) may be preferred to the [Dn.n] pattern, see the discussion of [Dn.pr].
    • Most verbs in this pattern can be made passive, with the indirect object becoming the subject:
    (6.56)
    a.
    The Prime Minister has offered commonwealth governments every facility possible to safeguard their interests.
    b.
    Commonwealth governments have been offered (by the Prime Minister) every facility possible to safeguard their interests.

    There are exceptions where no passive is possible [Dn.n no passive].

    A passive in which the direct object becomes subject is rare [search: NP-LGS $ NP-OB2]:

    (6.57)
    Every facility possible to safeguard their interests has been offered commonwealth governments (by the Prime Minister).

    A case where the direct object is missing:

    (6.58)
    =N(" a   friend    asked him once ")
    "PRN","IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","a"
    "PRN","IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","N","friend"
    "PRN","IP-MAT","VBD;~Dn.w","asked"
    "PRN","IP-MAT","NP-OB2","PRO","him"
    "PRN","IP-MAT","ADVP-TMP","ADV","once"
    "ID","12#75_susanne_N11@7,9"

    6.5.9   [Dn.pr]

    [Dn.pr] is the code to indicate clause structure where there is a ditransitive verb + direct object that is a noun phrase + indirect object that is a preposition phrase.

    Table 6.29: [Dn.pr]

    subjectditransitive verbdirect object: noun phraseindirect object: to/for + noun phrase
    1.The Prime Ministerhas offeredevery facility possibleto commonwealth governments.
    2.Some major organisationsgiveadditional compensationto staff.
    3.The old chaptaughta great dealto him.
    4.The local centre for the handicappedlenta wheelchairto them.
    5.Theyfounda great beltfor her.
    6.I'll makea nice cup of teafor you.
    7.Daddyboughta new bikefor me.
    • In this pattern, the indirect object is placed at the end. It consists of to or for and a noun phrase.
    • When introduced by to, the indirect object refers to a person or people receiving something (see Dn.n, note (b)). When introduced by for, it refers to a person or people intended to benefit (see Dn.n, note (c)).
    • When the indirect object provides new information (e.g. in answer to a question), this pattern is preferred to [Dn.n], and the main stress falls on the last noun or pronoun.
    (6.59)
    Q: Who did Henri teach French to?
    A: Henri taught French to the `children. [Dn.pr]

    But when the direct object refers to information that is new, Dn.n is preferred:

    (6.60)
    Q: What did Henri teach the children?
    A: Henri taught the children `French. [Dn.n]
    • Most verbs in this pattern can be made passive, with the direct object of the active pattern becoming the subject of the passive one:
    (6.61)
    a.
    Father bought the white cat for Emma.
    b.
    The white cat was bought for Emma (by Father).

    There are exceptions where no passive is possible [Dn.pr no passive].

    The noun phrase direct object can appear after the preposition phrase indirect object, typically when it has more weight:

    (6.62)
    =N(" Through the Double Cross Committee <comma> usually known as the XX Committee <comma> these turned spies sent back to Germany a steady stream of fictitious information garnished with sufficient truth to give it credibility <comma> which the Germans apparently accepted without question . ")

    "IP-MAT","PP-MNR","NP","NML","NPR","Double"

    "IP-MAT","PP-MNR","NP","PU","<comma>"

    "IP-MAT","PP-MNR","NP","IP-PPL","PP-PRD","NP","NPR","XX"
    "IP-MAT","PP-MNR","NP","IP-PPL","PP-PRD","NP","N","Committee"
    "IP-MAT","PU","<comma>"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","these"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","IP-PPL","VVN","turned"

    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","PP","NP","IP-PPL","PP-MNR","P-ROLE","with"

    6.5.10   [Dn.f]

    Table 6.30: [Dn.f]

    subjectditransitive verbindirect object: noun phrasedirect object: that-clause
    1..
    2..
    3..
    4..
    • This pattern has an indirect object without to and a direct object consisting of a that clause (Cf Tf). The indirect object refers to the person or people addressed by the subject.
    • Some verbs in this pattern can be made passive, with the indirect object of the active pattern becoming the subject of the passive one:
    (6.63)
    a.
    Colleagues told Paul that the job wouldn't be easy.
    b.
    Paul was told (by colleagues) that the job wouldn't be easy.

    6.5.11   [Dpr.f]

    Table 6.31: [Dpr.f]

    subjectditransitive verbindirect object: to + noun phrasedirect object: that-clause
    1..
    2..
    3..
    • This pattern has an indirect object with to and a direct object consisting of a that clause (Cf Tf).
    • Some verbs in this pattern can be made passive. (Note the construction with it.)
    (6.64)
    a.
    The garage explained to customers that the spare parts had not been delivered.
    b.
    It was explained to customers (by the garage) that the spare parts had not been delivered.

    6.5.12   [Dn.w]

    Table 6.32: [Dn.w]

    subjectditransitive verbindirect object: noun phrasedirect object: finite clause/non-finite clause with wh
    1..
    2..
    3..
    4..
    • In this pattern, the direct object is a finite or non-finite clause beginning with

    EITHER (i) A wh-element, which can be a pronoun (who(m), whose, which, what), or a determiner + noun (which roads, what time), or an adverb (why (finite clauses only), when, where, how);

    OR (ii) One of the conjunctions if (finite clauses only) or whether (Cf Tw).

    • Some verbs used in this pattern can be made passive, with the indirect object of the active pattern becoming the subject of the passive one:
    (6.65)
    a.
    A friendly guard showed the prisoner how he could escape/how to escape.
    b.
    The prisoner was shown how he could escape/how to escape (by a friendly guard).

    6.5.13   [Dpr.w]

    Table 6.33: [Dpr.w]

    subjectditransitive verbindirect object: to + noun phrasedirect object: finite clause/non-finite clause
    1..
    2..
    • This pattern has an indirect object with to and a direct object consisting of a finite or non-finite wh-clause (Cf Tw). The indirect object refers to the person or people addressed by the subject.
    • Some verbs in this pattern can be made passive. (Note the construction with it.)
    (6.66)
    It was explained to the staff how they should handle complaints/how to handle complaints.

    6.5.14   [Dn.t]

    Table 6.34: [Dn.t]

    subjectditransitive verbindirect object: noun phrasedirect object: non-finite clause (to infinitive clause)
    1.Itpermitsthe stateto take over.
    2.Shetoldhimto play the piano.
    3..
    4..
    • In this pattern, the direct object is a non-finite clause, consisting of or containing a to-infinitive (Cf Tt).
    • Some verbs in this pattern can be made passive, with the indirect object of the active pattern becoming the subject of the passive one:
    (6.67)
    a.
    John and Mary encouraged Simon to stay.
    b.
    Simon was encouraged to stay (by John and Mary).

    6.5.15   [Dpr.t]

    Table 6.35: [Dpr.t]

    subjectditransitive verbindirect object: to + noun phrasedirect object: non-finite clause (to infinitive clause)
    1..
    2..
    3..
    4..
    • This pattern has an indirect object with to and a direct object consisting of or containing a to-infinitive (Cf Tt). The indirect object refers to the person or people to whom the subject is calling or signalling.
    • The verbs in this pattern cannot usually be made passive.
