    6.8   Verb sequences with auxiliaries in the clause

    • HAVE is used to form the perfect construction (see section 6.8.1)
    • BE is used to form either the progressive construction (see section 6.8.2) or the passive construction (see section 6.8.3)

      † aspectual HAG is limited to occurring with adverbial clauses (e.g. When having granted authorization, the competent minister shall publicly notice that effect.)

    We have so far considered basic clause structure that contains only a single main verb. This section adds the possibility of auxiliaries occuring in clause structure to form verb sequences. The annotation includes IP-PPL-CAT layers as clause intermediate structure to assist with isolating the components that create verb sequences and their clause contribution.

    Table 6.37: Tag for verb sequence layer

    IP-PPL-CATlayer for verb sequence

    Auxiliaries include forms such as will, have, and be. In a clause with a lexical verb, the presence of auxiliaries is optional. If present, they precede the main verb. Moreover, the different auxiliaries perform four different functions (see Table 6.38), and, when part of a tensed clause, these four different functions can join in sequences to allow twenty-four different combinations:

    Table 6.38: Combinations of a modal, catenative HAVE, catenative BE, and ending with a lexical verb

    NP-SBJ Modal (MD) Aspectual verbs Passive Lexical verb
    Perfect Progressive
    1 A ship sank (VBD;~I)
    2 A ship sinks (VBP;~I)
    3 A ship may sink (VB;~I)
    4 A ship had (HVD;~cat_e) †sunk (VVN;~I)
    5 A ship has (HVP;~cat_e) †sunk (VVN;~I)
    6 A ship was (BED;~cat_g) †sinking (VAG;~I)
    7 A ship is (BEP;~cat_g) †sinking (VAG;~I)
    8 A ship was (BED;~cat_e) †‡sunk (VVN;~Tn)
    9 A ship is (BEP;~cat_e) †‡sunk (VVN;~Tn)
    10 A ship may have (HV;~cat_e) †sunk (VVN;~I)
    11 A ship may be (BE;~cat_g) †sinking (VAG;~I)
    12 A ship may be (BE;~cat_e) †‡sunk (VVN;~Tn)
    13 A ship had (HVD;~cat_e) †been (BEN;~cat_g) †sinking (VAG;~I)
    14 A ship has (HVP;~cat_e) †been (BEN;~cat_g) †sinking (VAG;~I)
    15 A ship had (HVD;~cat_e) †been (BEN;~cat_e) †‡sunk (VVN;~Tn)
    16 A ship has (HVP;~cat_e) †been (BEN;~cat_e) †‡sunk (VVN;~Tn)
    17 A ship was (BED;~cat_g) †being (BAG;~cat_e) †‡sunk (VVN;~Tn)
    18 A ship is (BEP;~cat_g) †being (BAG;~cat_e) †‡sunk (VVN;~Tn)
    19 A ship may have (HV;~cat_e) †been (BEN;~cat_g) †sinking (VAG;~I)
    20 A ship may have (HV;~cat_e) †been (BEN;~cat_e) †‡sunk (VVN;~Tn)
    21 A ship may be (BE;~cat_g) †being (BAG;~cat_e) †‡sunk (VVN;~Tn)
    22 A ship had (HVD;~cat_e) †been (BEN;~cat_g) †being (BAG;~cat_e) †‡sunk (VVN;~Tn)
    23 A ship has (HVP;~cat_e) †been (BEN;~cat_g) †being (BAG;~cat_e) †‡sunk (VVN;~Tn)
    24 A ship may have (HV;~cat_e) †been (BEN;~cat_g) †being (BAG;~cat_e) †‡sunk (VVN;~Tn)

      † triggers IP-PPL-CAT layer creation in the clause annotation

      ‡ needs a (possibly zero) logical subject (-LGS) as a sister in the clause annotation

    The column layout of Table 6.38 shows that the verbs of a verb sequence occur in a fixed order. For example, may have sunk, and had been sinking are possible combinations. Furthermore, prior elements determine the form of following elements. This leads to combinations that keep to the rules of Table 6.39 when there is a lexical verb (VBD, VBP, VB, VVN, VAG) as the obligatory main verb that ends the sequence. Table 6.39 also notes (with †) how, in a tensed clause, the first word in a verb sequence is always a modal or tensed verb form (either MD, HVD, HVP, BED, BEP, VBD, or VBP).

    Table 6.39: Rules for forming combinations of a modal, catenative HAVE, catenative BE, and ending with a lexical verb

    Function Class of verb Following verb form
    Modal Modal verb (†MD) Infinitive (HV, BE or VB)
    Perfect aspect HAVE (†HVD;~cat_e, †HVP;~cat_e, HV;~cat_e) {en} participle (BEN or VVN)
    Progressive aspect BE (†BED;~cat_g, †BEP;~cat_g, BE;~cat_g, BEN;~cat_g) {ing} participle (BAG or VAG)
    Passive voice BE (†BED;~cat_e, †BEP;~cat_e, BE;~cat_e, BEN;~cat_e, BAG;~cat_e) {en} participle (VVN;~T* or VVN;~C* or VVN;~D*)
    Lexical verb Verb (†VBD, †VBP, VB, VVN, VAG)

      † initial element of a verb sequence for a tensed clause

        If we want to construct a verb sequence with perfect aspect followed by the main verb, the rules of Table 6.39 say that the main verb must be a {en} participle form: e.g. has sunk. The annotation of (6.76) illustrates the introduction of an IP-PPL-CAT layer into the clause structure following the ‘†’ indications of Table 6.38.

    (6.76)
    =N(" A ship had sunk ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","A"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","N","ship"
    "IP-MAT","HVD;~cat_e","had"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","VVN;~I","sunk"
    "ID","4_samples_ship@10,9"

        If we want to construct more complex sequences such as a modal verb followed by perfect aspect followed by progressive aspect followed by passive voice followed by a main werb, the rules of Table 6.39 say that the perfect aspect must be an infinitive form; the progressive aspect, an {en} participle form; the passive voice, an {ing} participle form; and the main verb, an {en} participle form: e.g. may have been being sunk. The annotation of (6.77) illustrates the introduction of three IP-PPL-CAT layers into the clause structure following the ‘†’ indications of Table 6.38.

    (6.77)
    =N(" A ship may have been being sunk ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","D","A"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","N","ship"
    "IP-MAT","MD","may"
    "IP-MAT","HV;~cat_e","have"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","BEN;~cat_g","been"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-PPL-CAT","BAG;~cat_e","being"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-LGS","*"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-PPL-CAT","VVN;~Tn","sunk"
    "ID","24_samples_ship@20,11,14,17"

    Note that the annotation of (6.77) also includes the zero marking (NP-LGS *), following the ‘‡’ indications of Table 6.38, to mark the clause as passive.

    6.8.1   Perfect construction

    The perfect form of a verb generally calls attention to the consequences of a prior event; for example, (6.78a) implies that he is still away, in contrast with (6.78b).

    (6.78)
    a.
    He has gone to lunch.
    b.
    He went to lunch.

    ‘Had gone to lunch’ takes a past time point (i.e. when we arrived) as its reference point and is another way of establishing time relations in a text. The perfect tense is formed by:

    • turning the verb into its past participle inflection
    • adding a form of the verb HAVE before it.

    This places multiple verbs in sequence, giving an IP-PPL-CAT layer in the annotation, as seen in (6.79) (= annotation for (6.78a)).

    (6.79)
    =N(" Mr. Yamada has gone to Tokyo . ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","N","Mr."
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","NPR","Yamada"
    "IP-MAT","HVP;~cat_e","has"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","VVN","gone"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-DIR","P-ROLE","to"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-DIR","NP","NPR","Tokyo"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "ID","1366_samples_kisonihongo@10,9"

    The perfect construction can also be combined with the progressive (e.g. he has been going).

    6.8.2   Progressive construction

    The progressive (also known as the ‘continuous’) form of a verb generally describes events in progress. It is formed by combining the verb's present participle (e.g. watching) with a form of the verb BE (e.g. I was watching). The progressive can also be combined with the perfect (e.g. Bill has been watching).

    (6.80)
    =N(" I was watching the fire-engines ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
    "IP-MAT","BED;~cat_g","was"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","VAG","watching"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","D","the"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","NS","fire-engines"
    "ID","391_christine_T40@8,5"
    (6.81)
    =N(" is there heat coming out anywhere ")
    "CP-QUE","IP-SUB","BEP;~cat_g","is"
    "CP-QUE","IP-SUB","EX","there"
    "CP-QUE","IP-SUB","NP-ESBJ","N","heat"
    "CP-QUE","IP-SUB","IP-PPL-CAT","VAG;~Ip","coming"
    "CP-QUE","IP-SUB","IP-PPL-CAT","ADVP-DIR","RP","out"
    "CP-QUE","IP-SUB","IP-PPL-CAT","ADVP-LOC","ADV","anywhere"
    "ID","116_christine_T12@3,10"
    (6.82)
    =N(" Bill has been watching little Alice ever since Mary left . ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","NPR","Bill"
    "IP-MAT","HVP;~cat_e","has"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","BEN;~cat_g","been"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-PPL-CAT","VAG","watching"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","ADJP","ADJ","little"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","NPR","Alice"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-SCON-CNT","ADVP","ADV","ever"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-SCON-CNT","P-CONN","since"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-ADV","NP-SBJ","NPR","Mary"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-ADV","VBD","left"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "ID","447_samples_blue_book@11,5,8"

    6.8.3   Voice

    Table 6.40: Tags for marking a logical subject

    NP-LGSlogical subject noun phrase
    PP-LGSlogical subject preposition phrase

    The ACTIVE VOICE is the basic, unmarked form of the clause.

    The PASSIVE VOICE is the more marked form of the clause in which the -SBJ corresponds in meaning to an -OB1 or -OB2 (or very occasionally a -PRD) of a corresponding active clause.

    The sentence It was eaten by our dog is the passive of Our dog ate it. A passive is recognisable from:

    • the past participle form eaten
    • the normal object (it) turned into the subject
    • the normal subject (our dog) turned into an optional preposition phrase with by
    • the verb BE, or some other verb, such as GET.
    (6.83)
    =N(" She was given a new outfit by her boyfriend . ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","She"
    "IP-MAT","BED;~cat_e","was"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","VVN;~Dn.n","given"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","D","a"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","ADJP","ADJ","new"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","N","outfit"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-LGS","P-ROLE","by"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-LGS","NP","NP-GENV","PRO","her"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-LGS","NP","N","boyfriend"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "ID","39_samples_ENG1100@8,5,18"
    (6.84)
    =N(" She has given a new outfit to her boyfriend . ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","She"
    "IP-MAT","HVP;~cat_e","has"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","VVN;~Dn.pr","given"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","D","a"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","ADJP","ADJ","new"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","N","outfit"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-OBU","P-ROLE","to"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-OBU","NP","NP-GENV","PRO","her"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-OBU","NP","N","boyfriend"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "ID","38_samples_ENG1100@8,5,18"

    GET can be used in place of BE as an auxiliary verb to form passives. Does she ever get asked for her autograph? A pane of glass got broken.

    Chapter 7
    Indexing and other matters


    7.1   Introduction

    Prior to this chapter, all annotation has avoided the need for indexing. This chapter extends coverage to data where full analysis requires indexing. This includes discussions of displacement (section 7.2), right node raising (section 7.6), and extrapositioning (section 7.7). Such data involves structural analysis that places an element at one layer when really its content contribution is needed at another layer.

        The chapter also contains a full discussion of relative clauses (section 7.4) and it-cleft constructions (section 7.5).

        The chapter also clears up other loose ends, notably with discussions of elision (section 7.8), parentheticals (section 7.9), exceptional scope (section 7.10), a use for IML outside of conjunction (section 7.11), and anaphora (section 7.13).


    7.2   Displacement

    There is no need for indexing when displacement keeps to the same structural layer. For example, (7.1) illustrates displacement with its sentence initial object This disparity: The canonical position for the object of a clause with a [Cn.n] main verb is just after the main verb (Cf. section 6.5.2). Being a simple clause, (7.1) has only the one clause layer (namely, its IP-MAT), and so, despite the displacement, the object remains at the same structural layer as its verb.

    (7.1)
    =N(" This disparity Smith terms the <lsquo> rent <hyphen> gap <rsquo> . ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","D","This"
    "IP-MAT","NP-OB1","N","disparity"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","NPR","Smith"
    "IP-MAT","VBP;~Cn.n","terms"
    "IP-MAT","NP-PRD","D","the"
    "IP-MAT","NP-PRD","PUQ","<lsquo>"
    "IP-MAT","NP-PRD","N","rent"
    "IP-MAT","NP-PRD","PU","<hyphen>"
    "IP-MAT","NP-PRD","N","gap"
    "IP-MAT","NP-PRD","PUQ","<rsquo>"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "ID","13_young_adult_E97@2"

    Table 7.1: Indexed *ICH* null element marking

    *ICH*abbreviation mnemonic for ‘interpret constituent here’

        When displacement crosses structural layers, indexing with *ICH* of Table 7.1 is needed to mark the point where the function contribution is needed, as (7.2) illustrates (again with a sentence initial object).

    (7.2)
    =N(" The story which Fred published in 1923 he had written before 1910 . ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-143","D","The"
    "IP-MAT","NP-143","N","story"
    "IP-MAT","NP-143","IP-REL","NP-OB1","RPRO","which"
    "IP-MAT","NP-143","IP-REL","NP-SBJ;{PERSON}","NPR","Fred"
    "IP-MAT","NP-143","IP-REL","VBD;~Tn","published"
    "IP-MAT","NP-143","IP-REL","PP-TMP","P-ROLE","in"
    "IP-MAT","NP-143","IP-REL","PP-TMP","NP","NUM","1923"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{PERSON}","PRO","he"
    "IP-MAT","HVD;~cat_e","had"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","*ICH*-143"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","VVN;~Tn","written"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-TMP","P-ROLE","before"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-TMP","NP","NUM","1910"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "ID","691_samples_blue_book@28,2"

    While the sentence initial object of (7.2) occurs internally to a sentence with a single clause, this clause contains a verb sequence that places the main verb inside a distinct IP-PPL-CAT layer. Annotation for the object role contribution needs to occur at the layer of the main ([Tn]) verb: This is accomplished with the placement of the object role information within the IP-PPL-CAT layer together with an instance of *ICH* that shares an index with the displaced object. The displaced object carries no function information itself, but rather its function marking resides directly above the *ICH* with which it shares its index. Note that the subject, while also occuring outside the IP-PPL-CAT layer, does not need a similar treatment, since the subject contribution of a main verb in a verb sequence is automatically inherited from the configuration of control.

    (7.3)
    =N(" hundred and twenty thousand I think it <apos>s on ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-221","NML","NML","NP","N","hundred"
    "IP-MAT","NP-221","NML","NML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
    "IP-MAT","NP-221","NML","NML","CONJP","NP","NUM","twenty"
    "IP-MAT","NP-221","N","thousand"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
    "IP-MAT","VBP","think"
    "IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ","PRO","it"
    "IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","BEP","<apos>s"
    "IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","PP-LOC","P-ROLE","on"
    "IP-MAT","CP-THT-OB1","IP-SUB","PP-LOC","NP","*ICH*-221"
    "ID","375_christine_T07@2,31"
    (7.4)
    =N(" Just when you think you <apos>ve got it straight <comma> along comes the Fool with his pig <apos>s bladder and whops you on the nose . ")
    "IP-MAT","PP-SCON-TMP","ADVP-MOD","ADV","Just"

    7.3   Displacement in questions


    7.4   Relative clauses

    Typically found within a noun phrase, a relative clause is a clause that is itself a modifier of an external head with the further property that the modified external head contributes a role within the relative clause. This necessitates the wherewithal to integrate the internal role. Such integration typically occurs in one of two ways:

    (i)
    with a relative pronoun (RPRO or RD) of which there are various kinds with varying placement requirements, or
    (ii)
    through the complete absence of a nevertheless selected element internal to the relative clause, represented in the annotation with a trace (*T*).

    An example of a further option for integration, although rare in English, is to have a pronoun (PRO) integrating the content of the modified head, called a resumptive pronoun.

    IP-RELrelative clause
    IP-INF-RELinfinitival relative clause
    IP-EOPempty operator clause
    IP-CARclause adjoined relative clause
    (7.5)
    =N(" reason I <apos>ve got a big box ")
    "NP","N","reason"
    "NP","IP-REL","NP-CNT","*T*"
    "NP","IP-REL","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
    "NP","IP-REL","HVP;~cat_e","<apos>ve"
    "NP","IP-REL","IP-PPL-CAT","VVN","got"
    "NP","IP-REL","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","D","a"
    "NP","IP-REL","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","ADJP","ADJ","big"
    "NP","IP-REL","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1","N","box"
    "ID","2#62_christine_T16@5"
    (7.6)
    =N(" problems for the body to tackle ")
    "NP","NS","problems"
    "NP","IP-INF-REL","NP-OB1","*T*"
    "NP","IP-INF-REL","P-CONN","for"
    "NP","IP-INF-REL","NP-SBJ","D","the"
    "NP","IP-INF-REL","NP-SBJ","N","body"
    "NP","IP-INF-REL","TO","to"
    "NP","IP-INF-REL","VB","tackle"
    "ID","24#13_young_adult_E63@5"

    stacking relative clauses versus relative clauses with coordination

    7.5   IP-CLF (it-cleft)

    -FOCtag extension marking the focus of a cleft construction
    IP-CLFfocus complement of cleft construction
    NP-CSBJcleft it noun phrase

    A clause can be clefted, i.e. divided into two parts, each with its own verb. The focus of a cleft construction is labeled with the FOC tag extension. The focus complement is labelled IP-CLF.

    (7.12)
    =N(" <ldquo> But it was from here that the firing came <comma> <rdquo> Tance murmured . ")
    "IP-MAT","PUQ","<ldquo>"
    "IP-MAT","CONJ","But"
    "IP-MAT","NP-CSBJ","PRO","it"
    "IP-MAT","BED;~cleft","was"
    "IP-MAT","PP-FOC","P-ROLE","from"
    "IP-MAT","PP-FOC","ADVP","ADV","here"
    "IP-MAT","IP-CLF","NP-DIR","RPRO","that"
    "IP-MAT","IP-CLF","NP-SBJ","D","the"
    "IP-MAT","IP-CLF","NP-SBJ","N","firing"
    "IP-MAT","IP-CLF","VBD;~Ipr","came"
    "IP-MAT","PU","<comma>"
    "IP-MAT","PUQ","<rdquo>"
    "IP-MAT","PRN","IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{TANCE}","NPR","Tance"
    "IP-MAT","PRN","IP-MAT","VBD;~Tf","murmured"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "ID","255_classics_DICK_1952@9,26"

    cf. The firing came from here.

    (7.13)
    =N(" what was it you were so interested in ")
    "CP-QUE","IP-SUB","NP-FOC","WPRO","what"

    7.6   Right node raising

    Right node raising occurs when: (i) there is coordination, and (ii) there is an element to the right of the coordination that has a place of interpretation inside each of the conjuncts of the coordination. For example, (7.14) illustrates right node raising internal to a noun phrase.

    (7.14)
    =N(" direct contact with or consumption of food contaminated by rodent urine or fecal matter containing virus particles ")
    "NP","NML","NP","ADJP","ADJ","direct"
    "NP","NML","NP","N","contact"
    "NP","NML","NP","PP","P-ROLE","with"
    "NP","NML","NP","PP","NP","*ICH*-1"
    "NP","NML","CONJP","CONJ","or"
    "NP","NML","CONJP","NP","N","consumption"
    "NP","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","P-ROLE","of"
    "NP","NML","CONJP","NP","PP","NP","*ICH*-1"
    "NP","NP-1","N","food"
    "NP","NP-1","IP-PPL","VVN","contaminated"
    "NP","NP-1","IP-PPL","PP-LGS","P-ROLE","by"
    "NP","NP-1","IP-PPL","PP-LGS","NP","NML","NP","N","rodent"
    "NP","NP-1","IP-PPL","PP-LGS","NP","NML","CONJP;@2","NP","N","urine"
    "NP","NP-1","IP-PPL","PP-LGS","NP","NML","CONJP;@3","CONJ","or"
    "NP","NP-1","IP-PPL","PP-LGS","NP","NML","CONJP;@3","NP","ADJP","ADJ","fecal"
    "NP","NP-1","IP-PPL","PP-LGS","NP","NML","CONJP;@3","NP","N","matter"
    "NP","NP-1","IP-PPL","PP-LGS","NP","IP-PPL","VAG","containing"
    "NP","NP-1","IP-PPL","PP-LGS","NP","IP-PPL","NP-OB1","N","virus"
    "NP","NP-1","IP-PPL","PP-LGS","NP","IP-PPL","NP-OB1","NS","particles"
    "ID","10#72_texts_FACTBOOK_1_2010@25,12,23"

    7.8   Elision

    We have seen elision already with missing phrase heads in chapter 3. It is also possible for entire phrases to be elided under parallelism. For example,

    (7.30)
    =N(" we were dying laughing watching them <comma> but Mrs Smith was n<apos>t ")
    "IML","IML","NP-SBJ","PRO","we"
    "IML","IML","BED;~cat_g","were"

    "IML","IML","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-SCON-ABS","IP-PPL","VAG;~Tn","watching"
    "IML","IML","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-SCON-ABS","IP-PPL","NP-OB1;{STEPHEN+RUSSELL}","PRO","them"
    "IML","PU","<comma>"
    "IML","CONJP","CONJ","but"
    "IML","CONJP","IML","NP-SBJ;{MRS_S}","N","Mrs"
    "IML","CONJP","IML","NP-SBJ;{MRS_S}","NPR","Smith"
    "IML","CONJP","IML","BED;~cat_g","was"
    "IML","CONJP","IML","IP-PPL-CAT","NEG","n<apos>t"

        Elision can be found when there is a grammatical code that lacks support from its environment. For example, in (7.36) want is coded Tnt and yet there is no direct object non-finite clause and indirect object noun phrase in its clause of occurrence.

    "IP-MAT-OB1","FS","PU","#"
    "IP-MAT-OB1","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
    "IP-MAT-OB1","MD","<apos>ll"
    "IP-MAT-OB1","VB;~phr_Vn<=>p","take"
    "IP-MAT-OB1","NP-OB1","PRO","it"
    "IP-MAT-OB1","ADVP-DIR","RP","off"
    "IP-MAT-OB1","PP-SCON-CNT-CND","P-CONN","if"
    "IP-MAT-OB1","PP-SCON-CNT-CND","IP-ADV","NP-SBJ","PRO","you"
    "IP-MAT-OB1","PP-SCON-CNT-CND","IP-ADV","VBP;~Tnt","want"

    (7.39)

    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","you"

    7.9.1   Tag questions

    The annotation uses PRN in the description of tag questions. To find tag questions, you can use pattern (7.41):

    (7.41)
    /^PRN/ > /^IP-MAT/ < (/^CP-QUE/ < (/^IP-SUB/ <-1 /^NP-SBJ/))

    This looks for a PRN node that is itself immediately under an IP-MAT, and that has immediately under it a CP-QUE. Furthermore the CP-QUE needs to immediately dominate an IP-SUB that has as its last child an NP-SBJ. Thus, (7.41) returns example sentences like (7.42).

    (7.42)
    =N(" I mean <comma> Sirena last night was a marvel <comma> was n<apos>t she ? ")
    "IP-MAT","FRM","I_mean"
    "IP-MAT","PU","<comma>"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{SIRENA}","NPR","Sirena"
    "IP-MAT","NP-TMP","ADJP","ADJ","last"
    "IP-MAT","NP-TMP","N","night"
    "IP-MAT","BED;~Ln","was"
    "IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","D","a"
    "IP-MAT","NP-PRD2","N","marvel"
    "IP-MAT","PU","<comma>"
    "IP-MAT","PRN","CP-QUE","IP-SUB","BED","was"
    "IP-MAT","PRN","CP-QUE","IP-SUB","NEG","n<apos>t"
    "IP-MAT","PRN","CP-QUE","IP-SUB","NP-SBJ;{SIRENA}","PRO","she"
    "IP-MAT","PU","?"
    "ID","33_texts_TED_talk_11@24,1,25,26,31"

    7.10   Exceptional scope

    Quantificational elements typically scope with a leftward direction, with the exception that intermediate layers, phrase heads, and verbs of clauses are always within the scope of other elements of the same layer. But a quantificational element with a functional role at its point of occurrence can also have broader scope consequences. This is the opposite of what happens for displacement in section 7.2. Displacement of a quantificational element had to capture the point of role integration while maintaining the scope gained from the place of occurrence. Exceptional scope needs to capture a broader scope contribution from an occurrence point that is the point of role integration.

    (7.43)
    =N(" Thousands of people are killed every year in explosions <comma> cave-ins and floods <comma> despite government crackdowns on safety violations . ")
    "IP-MAT","NP","*ICH*-1"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","NS","Thousands"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PP","P-ROLE","of"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PP","NP","NS","people"
    "IP-MAT","BEP;~cat_e","are"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-LGS","*"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","VVN","killed"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-TMP-1","Q","every"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-TMP-1","N","year"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-LOC","P-ROLE","in"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-LOC","NP","NML","NP","NS","explosions"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-LOC","NP","NML","PU","<comma>"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-LOC","NP","NML","CONJP;@3","NP","NS","cave-ins"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-LOC","NP","NML","CONJP;@4","CONJ","and"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-LOC","NP","NML","CONJP;@4","NP","NS","floods"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PU","<comma>"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-CNT","P-ROLE","despite"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-CNT","NP","N","government"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-CNT","NP","NS","crackdowns"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-CNT","NP","PP","P-ROLE","on"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-CNT","NP","PP","NP","N","safety"
    "IP-MAT","IP-PPL-CAT","PP-CNT","NP","PP","NP","NS","violations"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "ID","102_texts_Voice_of_America@2,20"

    7.11   Another use for IML

    Examples above have IP-PPL-CAT structures that follow the subject, so that the IP-PPL-CAT is within the scope of the subject. The word order of (7.44) suggests content for an IP-PPL-CAT (Opposing him) that precedes the subject (the French Admiral ...), and yet inheritance of the subject is also needed. Subject inheritance is achieved by the placement of the IP-PPL-CAT under an IML projection that also contains the auxiliary verb. With this annotation, the IML projection functions as a direct embedding of the layer that contains it, so that all other content of the containing layer scopes over the IML, as if the IML had its content placed last in the containing layer.

    (7.44)
    =N(" Opposing him was the French Admiral <comma> Jean de Vienne <mdash> a great sailor and an able strategist . ")
    "IP-MAT","IML","IP-PPL-CAT","VAG;~Tn","Opposing"
    "IP-MAT","IML","IP-PPL-CAT","NP-OB1;{GAUNT}","PRO","him"
    "IP-MAT","IML","BED;~cat_g","was"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","D","the"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","ADJP","ADJ","French"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","N","Admiral"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","PU","<comma>"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","PRN;@5","NP","NPR","Jean"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","PRN;@5","NP","FW","de"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","PRN;@5","NP","NPR","Vienne"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","PU","<mdash>"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","PRN;@7","NP","NML","NP","D","a"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","PRN;@7","NP","NML","NP","ADJP","ADJ","great"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","PRN;@7","NP","NML","NP","N","sailor"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","PRN;@7","NP","NML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","PRN;@7","NP","NML","CONJP","NP","D","an"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","PRN;@7","NP","NML","CONJP","NP","ADJP","ADJ","able"
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ;{DE_VIENNE}","PRN;@7","NP","NML","CONJP","NP","N","strategist"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "ID","6_lob_G01@3,2"

    It follows that an IML instance will either (i) have a placement that is directly under a clause layer, as in (7.44); or (ii) have a placement that is either sister to CONJP layer(s) or directly under a CONJP layer, as with the examples of clause internal coordination in section 4.4. An IML is never sister to another IML.

        In (7.45) IML is needed to ensure that in_order attaches at a higher scope level than without.

    (7.45)
    =N(" I spent the night at the library without sleeping in order to prepare for the exam . ")
    "IP-MAT","NP-SBJ","PRO","I"
    "IP-MAT","IML","VBD","spent"
    "IP-MAT","IML","NP-OB1","D","the"
    "IP-MAT","IML","NP-OB1","N","night"
    "IP-MAT","IML","PP-LOC","P-ROLE","at"
    "IP-MAT","IML","PP-LOC","NP","D","the"
    "IP-MAT","IML","PP-LOC","NP","N","library"
    "IP-MAT","IML","PP-SCON-MNR","P-CONN","without"
    "IP-MAT","IML","PP-SCON-MNR","IP-PPL2","VAG","sleeping"
    "IP-MAT","PP-SCON-CNT","P-CONN","in_order"
    "IP-MAT","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-INF","TO","to"
    "IP-MAT","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-INF","VB","prepare"
    "IP-MAT","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-INF","PP-OBU","P-ROLE","for"
    "IP-MAT","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-INF","PP-OBU","NP","D","the"
    "IP-MAT","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-INF","PP-OBU","NP","N","exam"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "ID","386_samples_purple_intermediate@5"

    7.12   Managing control

    Example (7.46) illustrates the need to mark IP-PPL2 and IP-INF2 to maintains subject control.

    (7.46)
    =N(" we should try to appreciate what is financially free whilst being prepared to pay emotional prices to achieve what is best for all ")
    "IML","NP-SBJ","PRO","we"
    "IML","MD","should"
    "IML","VB","try"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","TO","to"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","VB","appreciate"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","NP-OB1","IP-REL","NP-SBJ","RPRO","what"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","NP-OB1","IP-REL","BEP;~La","is"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","NP-OB1","IP-REL","ADJP-PRD2","ADVP","ADV","financially"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","NP-OB1","IP-REL","ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","free"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","P-CONN","whilst"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL2","BAG;~cat_e","being"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL2","IP-PPL-CAT","VVN","prepared"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL2","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-INF","TO","to"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL2","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-INF","VB","pay"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL2","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-INF","NP-OB1","ADJP","ADJ","emotional"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL2","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-INF","NP-OB1","NS","prices"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL2","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-INF","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-INF2","TO","to"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL2","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-INF","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-INF2","VB;~Tn","achieve"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL2","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-INF","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-INF2","NP-OB1","IP-REL","NP-SBJ","RPRO","what"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL2","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-INF","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-INF2","NP-OB1","IP-REL","BEP;~La","is"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL2","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-INF","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-INF2","NP-OB1","IP-REL","ADJP-PRD2","ADJS","best"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL2","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-INF","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-INF2","NP-OB1","IP-REL","PP-BNF","P-ROLE","for"
    "IML","IP-INF-OB1","PP-SCON-TMP","IP-PPL2","IP-PPL-CAT","IP-INF","PP-SCON-CNT","IP-INF2","NP-OB1","IP-REL","PP-BNF","NP","Q","all"
    "ID","60#35_young_adult_E05@9,30,36,47"

    In (7.47) the matrix subject controls through to the embedded be.

    =N(" The cost per job to the Exchequer was estimated to be around <pound> 30<comma>000 -LRB- National Audit Office <comma> 1990 <comma> 23-4 <comma> cited in Atkinson and Moon <comma> 1994 -RRB- . ")

        When there is clause-within-clause subordination, the possibility arises of an element from the main clause making a direct contribution to the subordinate clause while being left with unclear status in the main clause. One notable scenario of this involves derived subjects. (The other notable scenario involves derived objects.)

    (7.48)
    =N(" these are more likely to crease ")
    "IP-ADV","NP-DSBJ","D","these"
    "IP-ADV","BEP;~La","are"
    "IP-ADV","ADJP-PRD2","ADVP","ADVR","more"
    "IP-ADV","ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","likely"
    "IP-ADV","IP-INF-NSBJ","TO","to"
    "IP-ADV","IP-INF-NSBJ","VB","crease"
    "ID","15#101_bnc_B27@13,2"
    (7.49)
    =N(" the problems of the reform programme became more difficult to contain ")
    "IP-ADV","NP-DSBJ","D","the"
    "IP-ADV","NP-DSBJ","NS","problems"
    "IP-ADV","NP-DSBJ","PP","P-ROLE","of"
    "IP-ADV","NP-DSBJ","PP","NP","D","the"
    "IP-ADV","NP-DSBJ","PP","NP","N","reform"
    "IP-ADV","NP-DSBJ","PP","NP","N","programme"
    "IP-ADV","VBD;~La","became"
    "IP-ADV","ADJP-PRD2","ADVP","ADVR","more"
    "IP-ADV","ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","difficult"
    "IP-ADV","IP-INF-NSBJ","NP-LGS","*"
    "IP-ADV","IP-INF-NSBJ","TO","to"
    "IP-ADV","IP-INF-NSBJ","VB","contain"
    "ID","51#20_bnc_B20@25,2"

        Tag extension -NSBJ marking a notional subject can also be seen when the clause has: (i) a subject marked -ESBJ occurring with existential there, as in (7.50); or (ii) a subject inherited from control, as with the environment created by the reduced relative clause (the IP-PPL under NP) in (7.51).

    (7.50)
    =N(" There are bound to be demonstrations . <rquo> ")
    "IP-MAT","EX","There"
    "IP-MAT","IML","BEP;~La","are"
    "IP-MAT","IML","ADJP-PRD2","ADJ","bound"
    "IP-MAT","IML","IP-INF-NSBJ","TO","to"
    "IP-MAT","IML","IP-INF-NSBJ","BE","be"
    "IP-MAT","NP-ESBJ","NS","demonstrations"
    "IP-MAT","PU","."
    "IP-MAT","PUQ","<rquo>"
    "ID","19_lob_A01@15,10"
    (7.51)
    =N(" the increasing numbers of young intellectuals taking part and more importantly <comma> failing to return ")
    "NP","D","the"
    "NP","ADJP","ADJ","increasing"
    "NP","NS","numbers"
    "NP","PP","P-ROLE","of"
    "NP","PP","NP","ADJP","ADJ","young"
    "NP","PP","NP","NS","intellectuals"
    "NP","IP-PPL","IML","IML","VAG","taking"
    "NP","IP-PPL","IML","IML","NP-OB1","N","part"
    "NP","IP-PPL","IML","CONJP","CONJ","and"
    "NP","IP-PPL","IML","CONJP","ADVP-MOD","ADVP","ADVR","more"
    "NP","IP-PPL","IML","CONJP","ADVP-MOD","ADV","importantly"
    "NP","IP-PPL","IML","CONJP","PU","<comma>"
    "NP","IP-PPL","IML","CONJP","IML","VAG","failing"
    "NP","IP-PPL","IML","CONJP","IML","IP-INF-NSBJ","TO","to"
    "NP","IP-PPL","IML","CONJP","IML","IP-INF-NSBJ","VB","return"
    "ID","55#21_bnc_B20@40"

    Note the presence of the IML layer in (7.50). With this annotation, the IML projection functions as a direct embedding of the layer that contains it, so that all other content of the containing layer scopes over the IML, as if the IML had final placement within its containing layer. This allows the -ESBJ subject to scope into the IML.


    7.13   Anaphora

    (7.52)
    =N(" they were disappointed . And determined not to show it ")
    "IML","NP-SBJ","PRO","they"
    "IML","IML","IML","BED;~cat_e","were"
    "IML","IML","IML","IP-PPL-CAT;{DISAPPOINTMENT}","VVN;~Tn","disappointed"
    "IML","IML","IML","PU","."
    "IML","IML","CONJP","CONJ","And"
    "IML","IML","CONJP","IML","VBD;~Tt","determined"
    "IML","IML","CONJP","IML","IP-INF-OB1","NEG","not"
    "IML","IML","CONJP","IML","IP-INF-OB1","TO","to"
    "IML","IML","CONJP","IML","IP-INF-OB1","VB;~Tn","show"
    "IML","IML","CONJP","IML","IP-INF-OB1","NP-OB1;{DISAPPOINTMENT}","PRO","it"
    "ID","47#139_susanne_N05@9,27"
